Constitutive vs. Accidental Attributes and Infinite Regress
Author distinguishes constitutive and accidental attributes using triangle example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between constitutive and accidental attributes and explain why constitutive attributes cannot attach after the essence.

Core Idea
If an attribute were constitutive, it must be part of the essence before attachment, else infinite regress.

In this passage, the author of al-Mantiq al-Kabir differentiates between attributes that are constitutive (muqawwim) of an essence and those that attach after the essence is realized (accidents). Using the triangle example, he demonstrates that if an accidental attribute were constitutive, the essence would be composed of infinite constituents—an impossibility. This lesson breaks down that argument and its implications for defining essences in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of attributes
Find in the passage where the author mentions attributes that attach to the essence (mahiyya). Note the terms 'accident' ('arad) and the classification into particular/universal, necessary/separable.
The passage states that attributes attach after the essence is realized; they are not part of the essence itself.
2
Examine the triangle example
Read the author's example of a triangle: its properties (like the sum of angles) attach after its constitution by its sides. Identify what 'constitution by its sides' means—the sides are constitutive, while the properties are accidental.
The triangle's sides are its constitutive parts; its properties are not.
3
Apply the reductio argument
The author says: if those properties were constitutive, the triangle would have infinite constituents. Why? Because each property would require another property to attach, leading to an infinite chain. Understand that this is impossible, so the properties cannot be constitutive.
This is a logical proof by contradiction: assuming the opposite leads to absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يستراب في أنَّ ما يلحق الماهية من الأوصاف التي يسمونها أعراضًا فذلك إنَّما يلحق الماهيّة بعد تحققها؛ خاصًا كان ذلك العرض أو عاما لازمًا كان أو مفارقًا لحوق المثلث بعد التَّقوم بأضلاعه إذ لو كانت هي وأمثالها مقوّمات لكان المثلث وما يجرى مجراه مركباً من مقوّمات غير متناهية. فلو كان لزومها بغير وسط لكانت معلومة واجبة اللُّزوم فكانت ممتنعة الرفع، وإنْ كان بوسط فكذلك فإنه - أي الوسط - هو اللازم البين بلا وسط ؛ وإِلَّا يلزم التسلسل مقوَّمًا كان ذلك الوسط أو لم يكن مقوَّمًا. فإذن لا بدَّ وأنْ يكون من اللوازم إمَّا بوسط كما مرَّ أو لا بوسط . ولا يقال لا يكون ذلك الوسط لازما بينا إِلَّا وأنْ يكون العلم بالماهية علَّة العلم بلازمها القريب، وذلك ممتنع؛ وإلَّا لكانت اللوازم بأسرها معلومة بينة اللزوم. فإن كان قريبا من اللوازم
And there is no doubt that what attaches to the essence of the descriptions they call accidents, that attaches to the essence after its realization; whether that accident is particular or universal, necessary or separable, like the attachment of triangle after its constitution by its sides, for if they and their likes were constitutive, then the triangle and what proceeds in its manner would be composed of infinite constituents. So if their necessity were without a middle term, they would be known and necessarily necessitating, and thus impossible to remove, and if it were through a middle term then likewise, for it - the middle term - is the necessary concomitant that is evident without a middle term; otherwise infinite regress would follow, whether that middle term is constitutive or not. Therefore, there must be from the necessary concomitants either through a middle term as mentioned or without a middle term. And it cannot be said that the middle term is not an evident necessary concomitant unless knowledge of the essence is the cause of knowledge of its proximate necessary concomitant, and that is impossible; otherwise all necessary concomitants would be known with evident necessity. So if it is proximate of the necessary concomitants...
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage from al-Mantiq al-Kabir explores the nature of attributes ('arad) that attach to an essence (mahiyya). The author states that such attributes attach only after the essence is realized. They may be particular or universal, necessary (lazim) or separable (mufariq). He gives the example of the triangle: its properties (like the sum of angles) attach after its constitution by its sides. If those properties were constitutive, then the triangle would have infinite constituents, which is absurd. He then discusses the epistemology of necessary attributes: if a necessary attribute is known without a middle term (i.e., directly), it is known as necessitating and cannot be removed. If it is known through a middle term, then that middle term must itself be a necessary attribute that is evident without a middle term; otherwise, infinite regress would occur. Consequently, necessary concomitants must be either known directly or through a middle, but the middle must be directly evident. He refutes the objection that the middle term might not be evident by arguing that if knowledge of the essence caused knowledge of its proximate necessary attribute, that would imply all necessary attributes are known directly, which is false. The fragment 'So if it is proximate of the necessary concomitants' likely introduces a further conclusion, but the text cuts off.

Key Terms
ماهية
عرض
مقوم
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the triangle example illustrate?
A) Triangle's properties are separable from its essence.
B) If the triangle's properties were constitutive, the triangle would have infinite constituents.
C) The triangle's essence is its sides.
D) All attributes of a triangle are necessary.

Key Takeaway
Constitutive attributes cannot attach after the essence; otherwise infinite regress results, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the claim: 'The attribute of life (hayat) is constitutive of God's essence.' Using the argument from this lesson, explain why this claim leads to infinite regress and is therefore impossible according to the logic presented. (Refer to the triangle analogy.)

Necessary Concomitants and the Need for an Evident Middle Term
Author discusses epistemology of necessary attributes and necessity of an evident middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why there must be necessary attributes that are directly evident and why infinite regress of middle terms is impossible.

Core Idea
Every known necessary attribute must be directly evident or traced to one that is, else infinite regress.

This lesson explores the epistemology of necessary concomitant (lazim) attributes. The author argues that if a necessary attribute is known, it must be known either directly without a middle term or through a middle term that is itself directly evident. Otherwise, an infinite regress of middle terms would prevent any knowledge. He refutes an objection that threatens this principle, reinforcing the necessity of directly evident attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify two modes of knowledge for necessary concomitants
Find in the passage where the author says a necessary attribute is known either without a middle term (bila wasat) or through a middle term (bi wasat).
The middle term is an intermediate concept that explains why the attribute necessarily accompanies the essence.
2
Examine the condition on the middle term
The author asserts that if known through a middle term, that middle term must itself be evident without a middle term; otherwise, infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow.
This ensures that the chain of knowledge ends at a directly evident necessary attribute.
3
Understand the infinite regress argument
If every middle term required another middle term, then no attribute could ever be known. The author concludes that there must be necessary attributes that are directly evident.
This is a foundational epistemological principle in Islamic logic.
4
Analyze the refuted objection
The passage considers the objection: 'What if the middle term is not directly evident but the essence causes knowledge of its proximate necessary concomitant?' The author rejects this because it would imply all necessary attributes are known directly, which is false.
The objection is countered by showing its consequence contradicts reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يستراب في أنَّ ما يلحق الماهية من الأوصاف التي يسمونها أعراضًا فذلك إنَّما يلحق الماهيّة بعد تحققها؛ خاصًا كان ذلك العرض أو عاما لازمًا كان أو مفارقًا لحوق المثلث بعد التَّقوم بأضلاعه إذ لو كانت هي وأمثالها مقوّمات لكان المثلث وما يجرى مجراه مركباً من مقوّمات غير متناهية. فلو كان لزومها بغير وسط لكانت معلومة واجبة اللُّزوم فكانت ممتنعة الرفع، وإنْ كان بوسط فكذلك فإنه - أي الوسط - هو اللازم البين بلا وسط ؛ وإِلَّا يلزم التسلسل مقوَّمًا كان ذلك الوسط أو لم يكن مقوَّمًا. فإذن لا بدَّ وأنْ يكون من اللوازم إمَّا بوسط كما مرَّ أو لا بوسط . ولا يقال لا يكون ذلك الوسط لازما بينا إِلَّا وأنْ يكون العلم بالماهية علَّة العلم بلازمها القريب، وذلك ممتنع؛ وإلَّا لكانت اللوازم بأسرها معلومة بينة اللزوم. فإن كان قريبا من اللوازم

And it cannot be said that the middle term is not an evident necessary concomitant unless knowledge of the essence is the cause of knowledge of its proximate necessary concomitant, and that is impossible; otherwise all necessary concomitants would be known with evident necessity. Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would happen if every necessary concomitant required a middle term that is not itself directly evident?

The I'tibari Nature of the Three Modalities
Author argues modalities are i'tibari, not real.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why necessity, impossibility, and possibility are i'tibari matters and not real existents, and identify the key arguments for this position.

Core Idea
Modalities are relational, not real; they exist in the mind, not in external entities.

In classical Islamic logic, the three modalities—necessity, impossibility, and possibility—are not independent existents in the external world. Rather, they are i'tibari (relational/considerational) matters. This lesson unpacks the author's argument that these modalities have no existence in themselves (fi al-a'yan) and are best understood as mental considerations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three modalities
Locate the terms al-wujub (necessity), al-imtina' (impossibility), and al-imkan (possibility) in the Arabic passage. Note that the author groups them together as the subject of the discussion.
These are the foundational modal concepts in logic.
2
Understand the claim that they are not 'adamiyya
The author states that the truth (al-haqq) is that these are i'tibariyya, not simply 'adamiyya (negative matters). Read the phrase: 'The truth is that they are relational matters (i'tibariyya), so they have no existence in themselves in external entities.'
This refines the view that modalities are mere negations.
3
Analyze the argument about general possibility
The passage presents a hypothetical: if general possibility (al-imkan al-'amm) is the negation of impossibility, then it would be a positive (thubuti) matter. But it cannot be a genus for necessity because that would imply composition in the Necessary Being, leading to a contradiction.
This shows why possibility cannot be a real attribute.
4
Recognize the i'tibari conclusion
The author concludes that these modalities are i'tibari, meaning they have no independent existence in external reality. They are mental constructs used to analyze propositions and arguments.
This aligns with the broader Islamic philosophical tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(هـ) الخامس أن هذه الثلاثة وهو الوجوب والامتناع والإمكان ففيها من الكلام بأنَّها من الأمور العدمية كلا وجملة، أو لا من الأمور العدمية، والحق أنّها من الأمور الاعتبارية فلا وجود لها بنفسها في الأعيان. وقد قيل في الإمكان إنَّ العام منه عبارة عن سلب الامتناع والامتناع سلب محض فيكون الإمكان العام أمرًا ثبوتيا لكنَّه لا يمكن أن يكون جنسًا للوجوب بالذات. وللإمكان الخاص كذلك وإن كان مشتركًا بينهما ؛ إذ لو كان جنسا لكان امتياز الوجوب بالذات عن الإمكان بالفعل. وما يكون كذلك فهو مركب، والمركب ممكن لافتقاره إلى الغير وهو جزؤه فيكون الواجب ممكنا؛ هذا خلف. ثم لقائل أن يقول إنَّه سلب محض وإلا لا يكون محمولا على الإمكان الخاص، والإمكان الخاص سلب محض وإلا لكان ممكناً لذاته فيكون إمكانه زائدا عليه؛ ولزم التسلسل . وأما الخاص فإنه لا يمكن أن يكون موجودًا البتة؛ وذلك لأنه لا يخلو من أن يكون الممكن موجودا؛ وحينئذ يكون واجب الوجود فلا يكون ممكن العدم، أو معدوما؛ وحينئذ يكون واجب العدم فلا يكون ممكن الوجود. ومَنْ أصرّ على هذا فإنه لا يعترف بالإمكان الخاص إلَّا بالنسبة إلى الزمان المستقبل، كما أنَّ هذا التخصيص متّصف بصفة كونه حيا في الحال؛ ففي الاستقبال يمكن أن يبقى حيا ويمكن أن لا يبقى لكنه لا يكون واردا على الحكم بكون الشَّيء ممكنا في الحال والاستقبال؛ إذ الحكم عليه بالنظر إلى
(E) Fifth: Regarding these three, namely necessity, impossibility, and possibility, there is discussion about whether they are wholly negative matters or not. The truth is that they are relational matters (i'tibariyya), so they have no existence in themselves in external entities. And it has been said about possibility that the general one of them is an expression of the negation of impossibility, and impossibility is pure negation, so general possibility would be a positive matter, but it cannot be a genus for necessity per se. And similarly for specific possibility, even though it is common between them; for if it were a genus, then the distinction of necessity per se from possibility would be by actuality. And whatever is like that is composite, and the composite is possible due to its dependence on another which is its part, so the necessary would be possible; this is a contradiction. Then one may say that it is pure negation, otherwise it would not be predicated of specific possibility, and specific possibility is pure negation, otherwise it would be possible in itself, so its possibility would be additional to it, and a regress would follow. As for the specific, it cannot possibly exist at all; this is because it is not free from either that the possible exists; then it would be necessary of existence and not possible of non-existence, or it is non-existent; then it would be necessary of non-existence and not possible of existence. And whoever insists on this does not acknowledge specific possibility except with respect to future time, just as this particular thing is characterized by the attribute of being alive in the present; in the future it is possible that it remains alive and possible that it does not remain, but this does not apply to the judgment that a thing is possible in the present and future; for the judgment upon it with respect to
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage deals with the nature of the three modal concepts: necessity, impossibility, and possibility. The author states that they are not 'adamî (negative) matters in a simple sense, but rather i'tibârî (relational/considerational), meaning they have no independent existence in external reality. Then a specific argument is presented: general possibility is sometimes said to be the negation of impossibility, which is pure negation, making general possibility a positive concept. However, it cannot serve as a genus for necessity because the distinction would be by actuality, leading to composition in the necessary, which is impossible. Furthermore, if possibility were pure negation, it would not be predicated of specific possibility without implying a regress. The most striking point is the claim that specific possibility (imkan khas) cannot actually exist. The reasoning is that if a possible thing exists, it becomes necessary (since its existence is actual), and if it is non-existent, it becomes impossible (since its non-existence is actual). Therefore, specific possibility is only applicable to future contingents. The author gives the example of a living being: in the present it is actually alive, so it is not possible that it is dead now, but in the future it could be alive or dead. Hence, possibility is properly understood as a future modal concept. The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing the discussion.

Key Terms
الوجوب
الامتناع
الإمكان
الأمور الاعتبارية
الأمور العدمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why cannot general possibility be a genus for necessity?
A) Because general possibility is a negative concept
B) Because it would make the necessary composite and thus possible
C) Because possibility is not predicated of necessity
D) Because the author rejects the very idea of genus

Key Takeaway
Necessity, impossibility, and possibility are i'tibari (relational) matters, not real existents, as shown by the argument that treating them as real leads to contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Apply the author's reasoning to the concept of 'impossibility' (al-imtina'): if impossibility were a real existent, what problem would arise? Construct a brief argument showing why it must be i'tibari.

10 min
Why Specific Possibility Cannot Exist in Actuality
Author argues specific possibility cannot exist in actuality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that specific possibility (al-imkan al-khas) cannot actually exist and explain its implication for future contingents.

Core Idea
A possible thing cannot be actual; if it is, it becomes necessary relative to that state.

In the second part of the passage, the author presents a striking argument: specific possibility (imkan khas) cannot be found in actual existence. The reasoning is that if a possible thing exists, its existence is actual, making it necessary (relative to that state), and if it does not exist, its non-existence is actual, making it impossible. Thus, specific possibility applies only to future contingents. This lesson walks through that argument.

Guided Path
1
Locate the claim about specific possibility
Find in the Arabic the phrase 'وأما الخاص فإنه لا يمكن أن يكون موجودًا البتة' (As for the specific, it cannot possibly exist at all). This is the conclusion the author defends.
Specific possibility is the possibility of a specific thing.
2
Understand the exclusive disjunction
The author says a possible thing is either existent or non-existent. If existent, it is necessary (wajib al-wujud); if non-existent, it is impossible (wajib al-'adam). Write out the disjunction: either the possible exists (then it is necessary) or it does not exist (then it is impossible).
This is a logical disjunction that exhausts the options.
3
See why this eliminates actual specific possibility
Since in both cases the thing is either necessary or impossible, there is no room for it to be merely possible while it exists. So specific possibility cannot be a real property of an actual thing.
The argument shows that possibility is not a state of actuals.
4
Recognize the resolution: future contingents
The author notes that specific possibility is only valid for future time (al-zaman al-mustaqbal). Use his example: a living person is actually alive now, so it is not possible that he is dead now, but it is possible that he will be alive or dead in the future.
This shows that possibility is a future-oriented concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Furthermore, if possibility were pure negation, it would not be predicated of specific possibility without implying a regress. The most striking point is the claim that specific possibility (imkan khas) cannot actually exist. The reasoning is that if a possible thing exists, it becomes necessary (since its existence is actual), and if it is non-existent, it becomes impossible (since its non-existence is actual). Therefore, specific possibility is only applicable to future contingents. The author gives the example of a living being: in the present it is actually alive, so it is not possible that it is dead now, but in the future it could be alive or dead. Hence, possibility is properly understood as a future modal concept. The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing the discussion.

Key Terms
الإمكان الخاص
سلب محض
واجب الوجود
واجب العدم
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about the existence of specific possibility?
A) It exists only in the mind
B) It cannot exist at all
C) It exists in actuality for some things
D) It is a species of necessity

Key Takeaway
Specific possibility cannot be a real property of any actual thing; it applies only to future contingents, as actual existence renders it necessary or impossible relative to that state.

The Three Theoretical Sciences: Natural, Mathematical, Divine
Classifies sciences into practical and theoretical, then divides theoretical into natural, mathematical, and divine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given science into one of the three theoretical sciences based on its relationship to matter in external reality and in the mind.

Core Idea
The three theoretical sciences are defined by how they relate to matter.

In this passage, the author divides theoretical sciences into three categories based on the relationship of their subject matter to matter. This classification is foundational for understanding how different Islamic sciences (like kalam, physics, and mathematics) differ in their objects of study. Let's unpack the criterion and apply it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion for division
The author uses two aspects: whether the existence of the subject is related to matter 'in external reality' (fi al-kharij) and 'in the mind' (fi al-dhihn).
Both aspects must be considered together.
2
Apply to natural science
Natural science (al-'ilm al-tabi'i) deals with things that exist in matter both externally and in the mind. For example, a tree has matter both in reality and in our mental conception.
3
Apply to mathematical science
Mathematical science (al-riyadi) deals with quantities and shapes that exist in matter externally (e.g., a circular coin) but are abstracted in the mind (we can think of a perfect circle without matter).
Abstraction is mental only.
4
Apply to divine science
Divine science (al-ilahi) deals with existence that is not related to matter at all, neither externally nor mentally—such as God, angels, and abstract metaphysical concepts.
5
Rule out a fourth division
The author argues that no science can have its subject related to matter only in the mind and not externally, because if it is related in the mind, it must have some external basis. Hence, only three categories exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن علمنا بأن الكل أعظم من جزئه مثلا، لا يمكن أن يكون في الأزل على خلاف ما يكون في الأبد على أنه من حيث هو هو ينقسم إلى أقسام مختلفة، فإن منها ما يكون مطلوبًا لا لذاته بل للغير وذلك أن يكون معونة لتحصيل ما عداه من العلوم وهو المنطق، ومنها ما يكون مطلوبًا لذاته. وذلك إما أن يكون علمًا بما لا يكون وجوده باختيارنا وإنه من العلوم النظرية، وإما أن يكون علما بما يكون وجوده باختيارنا وإنه من العلوم العملية؛ والعلوم العملية قد تسمى فلسفة عمليةً، والمقصود من النظرية اعتقاد رأي فقط، ومن العملية اعتقاد رأي لأجل العمل، فالنظرية أولى بأن تُنسب إلى الرأي وما يكون فيها من الموجودات. فذلك قد يكون من جملة ما يخالط الحركة نحو الإنسانية مثلا، وقد يكون من جملة ما لا يخالط نحو الملكية. والأول إما أن لا يمكن تجرده عن مادة معينة لا في القوام ولا في الوهم، وإما أن يمكن وذلك في الوهم لا في القوام. وبالجملة فالعلوم النظرية تنقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام؛ لأنه (أ) إما أن يكون وجوده متعلقا بالمادة في الخارج والذهن جميعًا وهو العلم الطبيعي، (ب) أو متعلقا بالمادة في الخارج لا في الذهن وهو الرياضي (ت) أو لا يكون متعلقا بالمادة لا في الخارج ولا في الذهن وهو الإلهي؛ ولا مجال للقسم الرابع فإن من المحال أن يكون متعلقا بالمادة في الذهن لا في الخارج.
For we know that the whole is greater than its part, for example, cannot be in pre-eternity contrary to what it is in post-eternity, but rather from the perspective of its essence it divides into different divisions. Among them is what is sought not for itself but for another, that is, it is an aid for attaining other sciences, and that is logic. And among them is what is sought for itself. That is either knowledge of that whose existence is not by our choice, and it is from the theoretical sciences; or knowledge of that whose existence is by our choice, and it is from the practical sciences. The practical sciences may be called practical philosophy. The intention of theoretical is only belief in an opinion, and of practical is belief in an opinion for the sake of action. So theoretical is more deserving of being attributed to opinion and what is in it of existents. That [theoretical] may be among what mixes with motion towards humanity, for example, and it may be among what does not mix, towards divinity. The first is either such that it cannot be abstracted from a specific matter neither in subsistence nor in imagination, or it can be abstracted in imagination but not in subsistence. In summary, the theoretical sciences divide into three divisions: because (A) either its existence is related to matter in both external [reality] and the mind, and it is natural science; (B) or it is related to matter in external [reality] but not in the mind, and it is mathematical science; (T) or it is not related to matter neither in external [reality] nor in the mind, and it is divine science. There is no room for a fourth division, for it is impossible for it to be related to matter in the mind but not in external [reality].
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a classification of sciences. The author begins by noting that some truths (like 'the whole is greater than its part') are eternal and unchanging. He then divides sciences into two main categories: those sought as means for others (like logic, which is a tool) and those sought for themselves (the sciences proper). The latter are further divided into theoretical (concerned with knowledge of things not under our control) and practical (concerned with actions under our control). Practical philosophy deals with action. The author then focuses on theoretical sciences, which can be either mixed with motion (like physics dealing with change) or unmixed (like metaphysics). Among those mixed, some cannot be abstracted from matter at all (natural science), while others can be abstracted in the mind but not in external reality (mathematical science). Finally, he presents the standard triple division: natural science (related to matter in both external and mind), mathematical (related to matter externally but not mentally abstracted), and divine science (related to neither). He rules out a fourth category, as it would require matter in mind but not externally, which is impossible.

Key Terms
العلوم النظرية
العلم الطبيعي
الرياضي
الإلهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Mathematical science
C) Divine science
D) Logic

Key Takeaway
The theoretical sciences are exhaustively divided into three based on whether their subject matter is related to matter in both external reality and the mind (natural), only externally (mathematical), or in neither (divine).

Distinguishing Word Meaning from Quiddity
Distinguishing word meaning from quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the concept of a word (lafz) and the quiddity (mahiyya) it signifies, using the example of 'ilm.

Core Idea
Word meaning and quiddity are not the same; one can be known without the other.

The author begins by clarifying a common confusion: that the meaning of a word and the quiddity of the thing it names are identical. He shows they are distinct, using the example of the word 'ilm. This distinction is foundational for understanding how definitions work in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The author states that one should not think that the concept (mafhum) of the word 'ilm and its quiddity (mahiyya) are one and the same. This is the starting point.
2
Find the two contrasts
The author gives two cases: (1) The concept of the word can be known without knowing the quiddity. (2) The quiddity can be known without knowing what the word indicates. Identify these in the Arabic.
Case 1: 'concept of the word may be known without knowledge of the quiddity'; Case 2: 'among quiddities there are those known without knowing what the word indicates'.
3
Apply the distinction to a real example
Consider the word 'ilm. Can you know the meaning of the word without knowing the essence of knowledge? Conversely, can you know the essence of knowledge without knowing that the word 'ilm refers to it? Reflect on how this affects definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن لفظ العلم لا يكون من الألفاظ المشتركة، ولا يظن أن مفهوم لفظ العلم وماهيته واحد. وقد كان مفهوم اللفظ من حيث مفهوم اللفظ قد يعلم بدون العلم بالماهية؛ وبالعكس كذلك فإن من الماهيات ما كان معلومًا للبعض من الأشخاص من غير أن يعلم بأن ما يدل عليه اللفظ ما هو؛ وهذا ظاهر . ثم من اللوازم أن يعلم بأن لكل شيء ماهية وماهية الشيء من حيث هي الماهية أعم بالنسبة إلى ماهية هي في الأعيان. وبالنسبة إلى ماهية هي في الأذهان كذلك. فيكون بها اعتبارات ثلاثة [1] اعتبار الماهية بما هي هي غير مضافة أصلا لا إلى الوجود الذهني ولا إلى الوجود الخارجي ولا إلى الغير مما يلحقها من حيث هي [ب] كذلك اعتبارها من حيث هي في الأعيان فيلحقها حينئذ من الأعراض ما يختص وجودها ذلك من حيث هي في الأعيان، [ت] واعتبارها من حيث هي في الأذهان فيلحقها حينئذ من الأعراض ما يختص وجودها ذلك مثل الوضع والحمل، ومثل الجزئية والكلية والذاتية والعرضية، وغير ذلك. فإنه ليس في الموجودات الخارجية ذاتية ولا عرضية في الحمل / فلا يكون البعض منها موضوعا ولا محمولا ولا مقدمة ولا قياسا ولا غير ذلك؛ وهذه كلها من جملة ما يعرف بالتأمل.
For the word 'ilm is not among ambiguous words, and one should not think that the concept of the word 'ilm and its quiddity are one. Indeed, the concept of the word, as a concept of a word, may be known without knowledge of the quiddity; and conversely, among quiddities there are those known to some persons without them knowing what the word indicates; this is clear. Then it is necessary to know that everything has a quiddity, and the quiddity of a thing as quiddity is more general relative to the quiddity that is in external objects, and likewise relative to the quiddity that is in minds. Thus it has three considerations: [1] consideration of the quiddity as it is in itself, not added at all to mental existence nor to external existence nor to others that attach to it as such; [2] likewise, consideration of it as it is in external objects, then there attach to it at that time the accidents that are specific to that existence as it is in external objects; [3] and consideration of it as it is in minds, then there attach to it at that time the accidents that are specific to that existence, such as position and predication, and such as particularity and universality, essentiality and accidentality, and other than that. For in external existents, there is neither essentiality nor accidentality in predication; thus some of them are not subjects nor predicates nor premises nor syllogisms nor other than that; and all these are among what is known by reflection.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents a key ontological doctrine: the three aspects of quiddity (mahiyya). First, the author clarifies that the meaning of a word (e.g., 'ilm) and its quiddity are distinct. One can understand the word without knowing the essence, and vice versa. Then, he states that every thing has a quiddity, and this quiddity can be considered in three ways: (1) in itself, abstracted from any existence; (2) as existing in external reality (a'yan); (3) as existing in the mind (adhhan). When considered in external reality, it acquires accidents specific to that realm (e.g., spatial location, physical properties). When considered in the mind, it acquires logical accidents such as being a subject or predicate, being universal or particular, being essential or accidental. The author emphasizes that these logical properties do not exist in external reality; they are mental considerations. This distinction is fundamental in Islamic logic and metaphysics.

Key Terms
الماهية
مفهوم اللفظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One can know the quiddity of something without knowing the word that indicates it.
B) One can never know the concept of a word without knowing its quiddity.
C) The concept of a word and its quiddity are always identical.
D) The word 'ilm is ambiguous (mushtarak).

Key Takeaway
The meaning of a word and the quiddity of the thing are two different things; each can be known independently of the other.

The Three Aspects of Quiddity
Explaining three aspects of quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three considerations of quiddity: in itself, in external existence, and in mental existence.

Core Idea
Quiddity can be considered in itself, externally, or mentally, each with its own accidents.

After establishing the distinction between word and quiddity, the author introduces the three ways quiddity can be considered: in itself, as it exists externally, and as it exists in the mind. He explains that different accidents attach to quiddity depending on which consideration is adopted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three considerations
In the Arabic, find the three numbered considerations: (1) quiddity as it is in itself (a), (2) as it is in external existence (t), (3) as it is in mental existence (th).
The letters [1], [ب], [ت] in the text correspond to these three.
2
List the accidents for each consideration
For external existence: accidents specific to that realm (e.g., spatial location). For mental existence: logical accidents like subject/predicate, universality/particularity, essential/accidental. For quiddity in itself: no accidents at all.
3
Understand why logical accidents are mental only
The author states that in external existents, there is no essentiality or accidentality in predication; these are mental considerations. This is crucial for avoiding reification of logical categories.

Defining Mufrad: When Parts Indicate Nothing
Author defines mufrad based on part non-indication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a word is mufrad or murakkab by analysing whether any of its parts indicate something in isolation or combination.

Core Idea
A simple word is one whose parts carry no meaning at all.

In this passage, the author introduces a fundamental distinction in logic and linguistics: the difference between simple (mufrad) and compound (murakkab) expressions. He shows that this distinction applies to both words and meanings, and that it is based on whether any part of the expression indicates something. The focus here is on the simple, defined by the total absence of part-indication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the domain of classification
Note that the author says mufrad and murakkab exist in both words and meanings. So the division is not limited to linguistic expressions.
2
Understand the fourfold division
The author lists four possibilities based on whether a part indicates in isolation, in combination, both, or neither. Write them in your own words.
The text enumerates: (1) only in isolation, (2) only in combination, (3) both, (4) neither.
3
Extract the definition of mufrad
Among the four, mufrad is defined as the one where the part indicates in neither state. The author explicitly says: 'al-mufrad ... huwa alladhi la yakunu dallan 'ala shay' ma hin huwa juz'uhu aslan'.
4
Apply to the given example
The author gives 'insan' (human). Check its parts: 'in' and 'san'. Do they mean anything in Arabic, either alone or combined? They do not, so 'insan' is mufrad.
5
Contrast with a compound example
Now think of a word like 'kitab' (book). Its part 'kaf' in isolation can mean 'like' (as a preposition in some dialects), so it would be murakkab because a part indicates in isolation.
The full definition of murakkab is not given here, but it is the opposite: a word whose part indicates in at least one state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المفرد والمركب فإنهما من جملة ما لا اختصاص له بالمعاني دون الألفاظ أو بالألفاظ دون المعاني، بل يوجد كل واحد منهما في كل واحدٍ منهما. وذلك في الألفاظ بحسب دلالة الجزء من اللفظ وبحسب لا دلالته كذلك في حالتي الانفراد والاجتماع، وذلك باعتبارها بين الحالتين على أربعة أقسام قسمة عقلية. فإن ما يدل بطريق المطابقة مثلا، فذلك لا يخلو من أن يكون جزؤه دالا على شيء ما في هذه الحالة فقط، أو في تلك الحالة كذلك، أو في هذه الحالة وفي تلك الحالة أيضًا، أو لا في هذه الحالة ولا في تلك الحالة كذلك. والمفرد منها هو الذي لا يكون دالا على شيء ما حين هو جزؤه أصلا كقولنا الإنسان، فـ «إن جزءًا ما منه وليكن إن» مثلا أو «سان» لا يدل على
As for the simple and the compound, they are among things that are not specific to meanings without words or to words without meanings; rather each of them exists in each of the two [words and meanings]. That is in words with respect to the indication of the part of the word and with respect to its non-indication likewise in the states of isolation and combination, and with respect to that between the two states [they fall] into four divisions by rational division. For what indicates by way of correspondence [for example], that is not free from its part indicating something in this state only, or in that state likewise, or in this state and in that state also, or neither in this state nor in that state likewise. The simple from among them is that which does not indicate anything when it is its part at all, like our saying 'al-insan', for 'a part of it, say 'in' or 'san', does not indicate
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage introduces the concepts of mufrad (simple) and murakkab (compound) as they apply to both words and meanings. The author explains that the distinction is based on whether a part of the expression indicates something. The division is rational, yielding four possibilities: (1) the part indicates in isolation but not in combination; (2) the part indicates in combination but not in isolation; (3) the part indicates both in isolation and combination; (4) the part indicates neither. The author then defines mufrad as the word whose part never indicates anything, such as 'insan' (human) where syllables like 'in' or 'san' have no meaning. The passage stops before the definition of murakkab, but the context implies that a compound is one whose part indicates something in at least one state. This classification is crucial for understanding how words convey meaning in logic and linguistics.

Key Terms

مركب
دلالة
جزء
مطابقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Only in meaning

Key Takeaway
A simple word (mufrad) is defined by the complete absence of indication from any of its parts in both isolation and combination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Four Ranks of Genus: Supreme, Intermediate, Lowest, and Isolated
Author classifies genus into four ranks: supreme, intermediate, lowest, and isolated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify any given genus into one of the four ranks (supreme, intermediate, lowest, isolated) using the criteria of whether it has a genus above it and/or a species below it.

Core Idea
Genus is not a single rank; it has four ranks based on hierarchical position.

In the previous lesson, we defined logical genus as a universal predicated of many things differing in species. But that definition is for genus taken absolutely. In reality, genus appears in different ranks depending on its place in the Porphyrian tree. In this passage, the author clarifies that there are four such ranks: supreme, intermediate, lowest, and isolated. Understanding these ranks is crucial for constructing accurate definitions and avoiding confusion in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of logical genus
The passage begins by warning that the previous definition is for logical genus, not a universal concept. Remind yourself that logical genus is a universal predicated of many things differing in species.
Logical genus is always relative to species.
2
Identify the four ranks and their criteria
The author lists four ranks based on two binary criteria: whether there is a genus above it, and whether there is a species below it. For each rank, note the combination: supreme (no genus above, species below), lowest (genus above, no species below), intermediate (both), isolated (neither).
The criteria are existence of superior genus and inferior species.
3
Analyze the examples given
The author provides examples: substance (jawhar) as supreme, animal (hayawān) as lowest, body (jism) and growing body (jism nāmi) as intermediate, and intellect ('aql) as isolated (if substance is not considered a genus). Examine why each example fits its rank.
The isolated genus example depends on whether substance is a genus for intellect.
4
Understand the argument about absolute genus
The author argues that 'absolute genus' cannot be a genus for all four simultaneously because only the intermediate genus can be a species of a higher genus. The supreme and lowest involve privation (absence of one relation), and the isolated involves two privations, so they cannot be species of an existential genus.
Privation (al-'adam) makes them unsuitable as species of a positive genus.
5
Apply the classification to new examples
Now try classifying other logical genera from Islamic sciences, such as 'jism' (body) which has a genus above (substance) and species below (animal, plant), so it is intermediate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And it should not be thought that the mentioned definition is a definition of genus absolutely; rather it is a definition of logical genus. And indeed among genera there are those that have four ranks, because it cannot be but that it is a genus with no genus above it but beneath it a genus – that is the supreme genus, like substance for example, and it is called the genus of genera. Or a genus with no genus beneath it but above it a genus – that is the lowest genus, like animal. Or a genus above it a genus and beneath it a genus – that is the intermediate genus, like body and growing body. Or a genus with no genus above it and no genus beneath it – that is the isolated genus, like intellect, if substance is not a genus. Then the absolute genus cannot be a genus for these four, for if it were a genus for the four, each one of them would be a species of that genus, and it is not possible for it to be so. Indeed, only the intermediate genus is fit for being a species, because the supreme genus is not realized except by two attributes one of which is privative, similarly the lowest. As for the isolated, that is not realized except by two privative attributes, and that which in itself includes privation is not fit to be a species for an existential thing, and it is necessary that all parts of an existential thing be existential. Commentary
This passage clarifies that the definition of genus given previously is specific to logical genus, not absolute genus. The author then enumerates four ranks of genus based on whether they have a superordinate genus and/or subordinate species. (1) Supreme genus (al-‘ālī) has no genus above it but has species below, e.g., substance (jawhar). (2) Lowest genus (as-sāfil) has a genus above but no species below, e.g., animal. (3) Intermediate genus (al-mutawassiṭ) has both a genus above and species below, e.g., body or growing body. (4) Isolated genus (al-mufrad) has neither genus above nor species below, e.g., intellect (if substance is not considered a genus). The author then argues that absolute genus cannot be a genus for all four simultaneously, because only the intermediate genus can be a species of a higher genus, since the supreme and lowest involve privation, and the isolated involves two privations, making them unfit to be species of an existential genus. The passage ends mid-sentence on 'then the intermediate, although it is fit...'

The Ten Categories

Why the Ten Categories Are Studied in Logic
Author explains the purpose of studying the ten categories in logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the ten categories are studied in Islamic logic and how they relate to the five universals and compound terms.

Core Idea
The ten categories are the scaffolding for building universal premises in syllogisms.

You have already learned about the five universals (the states of simple terms). Now, the author turns to a new set of states: the ten categories. But why are they needed? Not every state of simple terms is useful for constructing the compound terms that make up definitions, descriptions, and syllogisms. The categories are those special states that are indispensable for the logical composition of premises and arguments. This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Locate the stated purpose (الغرض)
Find the phrase 'والغرض منها' in the Arabic. This signals the author's goal for discussing the categories. It tells you what the section aims to achieve.
2
Relate the categories to the five universals
The author says: 'إذا علمت الأحوال الخمسة للألفاظ المفردة... فعليك أن تعلم أن معرفة هذه الأحوال مفيدة على معرفة الألفاظ المركبة.' He compares the previously studied five universals to the categories. Recognize that both are states of simple terms, but not all states are equally needed.
The five universals (genus, species, differentia, property, accident) are essential for defining terms. The categories are a different kind of classification.
3
Distinguish necessary from unnecessary states
The text states: 'ليس كل أحوال الألفاظ المفردة محتاجًا إليه في معرفة أحوال الألفاظ المركبة.' This means some states are irrelevant to building compound terms. The categories are among the beneficial ones. Understand the selectivity: logic only needs certain classifications to form valid arguments.
4
Connect categories to compound terms and syllogisms
The author explains that compound terms (propositions and syllogisms) depend on a 'طريق نافع' (beneficial way) for achieving assent (تصديق) and conception (تصور). This beneficial way comes through definitions, descriptions, and syllogisms, which are composed of premises. Categories ensure that these premises have universal subjects, allowing them to enter into scientific knowledge.
The categories are like the raw material for building universal premises—they guarantee that the terms are not particular but general enough for universal statements.
5
Grasp the requirement of universality
The passage ends with the requirement that premises have subjects that are 'كلية' (universal) and stand in essential or accidental relations. Categories provide the highest genera under which all things fall, ensuring the universality needed for syllogistic reasoning.
For example, the category 'substance' (جوهر) includes all individual substances, so a premise like 'All substances are created' uses a universal subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث : في المقولات العشر
والغرض منها أنك إذا علمت الأحوال الخمسة للألفاظ المفردة، فعليك أن تعلم أن معرفة هذه الأحوال مفيدة على معرفة الألفاظ المركبة، وأن تعتقد أن ههنا أحوالاً أخرى للألفاظ المفردة غير محتاج إليها معرفة الألفاظ المركبة؛ إذ ليس كل أحوال الألفاظ المفردة محتاجًا إليه في معرفة أحوال الألفاظ المركبة التركيب المقصود في المنطق. أما هذه فمما ينتفع بالوقوف عليه في صناعة المنطق؛ إذ الألفاظ المركبة إنما يكون تركيبها بحسب صناعة المنطق ليتوقف على الطريق النافع في مادة التصديق والتصور، وهذه الإفادة تَتِم بالفائدات وبالحدود والرسوم والقياسات، وإنها مؤلفة من مقدمات لا محالة. والمقدمات مفتقرة إلى أن تكون موضوعاتها كلية ليدخل في العلوم وأن تكون وموضوعاتها ومحمولاتها على النسب المذكورة في الذاتية والعرضية حتى
The third type: on the ten categories. And the purpose of them is that if you have known the five states of simple terms, it is incumbent upon you to know that knowledge of these states is useful for knowledge of compound terms, and to believe that here there are other states of simple terms not needed for knowledge of compound terms; for not every state of simple terms is needed in knowing the states of compound terms—the composition intended in logic. As for these (the categories), they are among that which benefits in the craft of logic; for compound terms are only composed according to the craft of logic in order to depend upon the beneficial way in the matter of assent and conception, and this benefit is completed by definitions and descriptions and syllogisms, and they are composed of premises inevitably. And the premises require that their subjects be universal to enter into the sciences, and that their subjects and predicates be according to the relations mentioned in essential and accidental (aspects), until...
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage introduces the third main section of the text: the ten categories (al-maqulat al-ashr). The author begins by stating the purpose (al-gharad) of studying them. He reminds the reader that after learning the five universal states of simple terms (al-ahwal al-khamsa), one must realize that knowledge of those states is useful for understanding compound terms—i.e., propositions and syllogisms. However, not all states of simple terms are needed for that purpose; some are irrelevant to the composition intended in logic. The ten categories are among the beneficial states for the craft of logic (sina'at al-mantiq). The reason is that compound terms in logic are composed according to a specific method that leads to beneficial knowledge in the domain of assent (tasdiq) and conception (tasawwur). This benefit is achieved through definitions (hudud), descriptions (rusum), and syllogisms (qiyasat), which are inevitably composed of premises. These premises must have universal subjects so that they can enter into the sciences, and their subjects and predicates must stand in the relations of essentiality (dhatiyya) and accidentally (aradiyya) as previously discussed. The passage thus sets the stage for the detailed treatment of the categories by highlighting their utility for logical analysis.

The Scope of Logic – What Is Necessary and What Is Not
Author argues that knowing the ten categories is not essential for logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between necessary and optional prior knowledge for learning logic as argued by the author.

Core Idea
The ten categories are optional baggage; only the five universals are essential.

This passage offers a sharp distinction between what a logician must know and what is merely extra. The author argues that the ten categories, often considered foundational, are in fact not required for mastering logical reasoning. Instead, the five universals (genus, species, differentia, property, accident) and their role in definitions and syllogisms are indispensable. This lesson helps you understand why the author draws this line and how it shapes the study of logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main claim
Read the passage and locate where the author explicitly states that knowledge of the ten categories is not necessary for logic. The key phrase is near the end: 'ولا من حال دلالتها على الأجناس العالية المسماة بالمقولات' – knowledge of the highest genera called categories is not needed.
The author mentions that a learner can proceed without ever thinking of the categories.
2
Extract the author's reasoning
The author gives several reasons: (1) Logic deals with compound terms (propositions, syllogisms) that depend on the five universals, not on categories. (2) Categories belong to other sciences like metaphysics, natural science, or linguistics. (3) Neglecting categories causes no significant defect ('لا يعرض من أعمال ذلك خلل يعتد به'). List these reasons in your own words.
3
Contrast with what is necessary
The author says knowledge of the five states of single terms (the five universals) is 'either necessary or nearly necessary' ('إما ضروري وإما كالضروري'). Identify why: they are used in definitions (from genera and differentiae), descriptions (from genera and properties), and syllogisms.
The five universals are tools for constructing definitions and demonstrations.
4
Understand the division of labor
The author assigns knowledge of the categories to other disciplines: first philosophy (metaphysics) for their existence, natural science for their concept, and linguistics for their names. The logician qua logician does not need to prioritize them. This shows that logic has a limited scope.
This prevents overloading the beginner with unnecessary ontology.

أخرى وهي دلالتها على الأمور الموجودة ولا ضرورة إلى معرفة تلك الأحوال أن يتعلم صناعة المنطق لا من جهة حال دلالتها على الأشخاص الجزئية، فإن ذلك مما لا ينتفع به في شيء من العلوم أصلا، فضلا عن المنطق. ولا The third type: On the ten categories. And the purpose of them is that when you know the five states of single terms, you must know that knowledge of these states is beneficial for knowledge of compound terms, and you must believe that here there are other states of single terms not needed for knowledge of compound terms; for not all states of single terms are needed in knowing the states of compound terms in the composition intended in logic. As for these, they benefit by pausing in the craft of logic; for compound terms are only composed according to the craft of logic to depend on the beneficial path in the matter of assent and conception, and this benefit is completed by beneficial propositions and by definitions and descriptions and syllogisms, and they are composed of premises necessarily. And premises require that their subjects be universal to enter into sciences, and that their subjects and predicates be on the relations mentioned in essentiality and accidentally until they enter into demonstration. And division is also among the ways leading to the acquisition of knowledge, as it is for genera to species by differentiae, preserving order so that no leap occurs from a degree to one not adjacent. And it may also be by properties and accidents. So knowledge of the five states is beneficial in syllogisms, and its benefit in definitions and descriptions is more apparent; for definitions are from genera and differentiae, and descriptions are from genera and properties. So presenting knowledge of these states that attach to single terms before starting in knowledge of compound terms is either necessary or nearly necessary. And single terms have other states, namely their signification of existing things, and there is no necessity to know those states when learning the craft of logic, neither from the aspect of their signification of particular individuals, for that is not beneficial in any science at all, let alone logic. Nor from the aspect of their signification of species, for the craft of logic is apart from that. And it suffices without it. Nor from the aspect of their signification of the highest genera called the categories. For the learner of logic, when he transitions after knowledge of single terms and nouns and words, it is possible for him to transition to learning propositions and their divisions, and syllogisms and definitions and their varieties, and demonstrative and non-demonstrative definitions, their genera and species. Even if it does not occur to him that there are ten categories or not ten, no significant defect arises from that. And that these are the things that are necessarily described as generic, and other things described as specific. Rather, knowledge of these: either from the aspect of the modality of existence, it belongs to first philosophy; or from the aspect of having a concept itself, it belongs to a part of natural science adjacent to first philosophy; and knowledge that they deserve words placed corresponding to them as primary or secondary position belongs to the craft of language. And it is not that the logician qua logician knows that more than other knowledge. Yes, there is one thing: the learner may benefit from this in a certain way.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological argument about the scope of logic. The author explains that while knowledge of the five universals (الأحوال الخمسة) is necessary or nearly necessary for learning logic, knowledge of the ten categories (المقولات العشر) is not. He begins by stating that the purpose of discussing categories is to clarify that there are other states of single terms that are not needed for compound terms. The compound terms in logic are composed according to the craft of logic and depend on beneficial paths in assent (تصديق) and conception (تصور). These are completed by benefits (al-fa'idat, i.e., beneficial propositions), definitions, descriptions, and syllogisms. Premises must have universal subjects to be part of sciences, and their subjects and predicates must be related essentially or accidentally to enter demonstration (برهان). Division (قسمة) also leads to knowledge, preserving order without leaps. Knowledge of the five universals is beneficial for syllogisms and more so for definitions and descriptions. Thus, learning these states is necessary or nearly necessary. The other states of single terms — their signification of particulars, species, and highest genera (the categories) — are not necessary for logic. A learner can proceed to propositions, syllogisms, and definitions without ever thinking of the categories. Knowledge of categories belongs to first philosophy ( metaphysics), natural science, or linguistics. The logician as logician does not need to prioritize them. However, there is one small benefit: a learner may gain some advantage from knowing categories. This passage refines the scope of logical study, warning against overloading the beginner with unnecessary ontology.

مقولات عشر
تصديق
تصور
برهان

The Five Universals vs. The Ten Categories – A Practical Distinction
Author argues that knowing the ten categories is not essential for logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the five universals are used in definitions and syllogisms, and why categories are irrelevant to those operations.

Core Idea
Definitions rely on genera and differentiae (five universals), not on the highest genera (categories).

This passage highlights the practical benefit of the five universals over the ten categories. The author shows that definitions and syllogisms — the core of logical demonstration — depend on the five universals. The categories, being the highest genera, are not needed for these operations. This lesson focuses on the concrete application of the five universals in logical reasoning, as opposed to the abstract and optional nature of the categories.

Guided Path
1
Locate the author's statement about definitions
Find where the author says definitions are from genera and differentiae (الحدود من الأجناس والفصول) and descriptions are from genera and properties (الرسوم من الأجناس والخواص). This directly ties definitions to the five universals (genus, differentia, species, property, accident).
The five universals include genus and differentia, which are the building blocks of definitions.
2
Connect the five universals to definitions and syllogisms
The author says knowledge of the five states is beneficial in syllogisms and even more so in definitions and descriptions. Understand that definitions require specifying the genus and differentia, while syllogisms rely on universal premises that use these universals.
For a syllogism to be demonstrative, its premises must be essential (dhatiyya) or accidental ('aradiyya), which again involve the five universals.
3
Show that categories are not needed
The author explicitly says that knowledge of the highest genera (categories) is not necessary. A learner can master definitions, syllogisms, and demonstrations without ever thinking about the ten categories. This shows that the categories are not tools for these operations.
Even if a student does not know the categories, no significant defect arises in his logical work.
4
Recognize the division of labor
The author assigns categories to other sciences: metaphysics (first philosophy) for their existence, natural science for their concept, and linguistics for their names. This clarifies that the logician's job is not to study categories but to use the five universals for reasoning.
This practical distinction prevents confusion between logic and ontology.

The third type: On the ten categories. And the purpose of them is that when you know the five states of single terms, you must know that knowledge of these states is beneficial for knowledge of compound terms, and you must believe that here there are other states of single terms not needed for knowledge of compound terms; for not all states of single terms are needed in knowing the states of compound terms in the composition intended in logic. As for these, they benefit by pausing in the craft of logic; for compound terms are only composed according to the craft of logic to depend on the beneficial path in the matter of assent and conception, and this benefit is completed by beneficial propositions and by definitions and descriptions and syllogisms, and they are composed of premises necessarily. And premises require that their subjects be universal to enter into sciences, and that their subjects and predicates be on the relations mentioned in essentiality and accidentally until they enter into demonstration. And division is also among the ways leading to the acquisition of knowledge, as it is for genera to species by differentiae, preserving order so that no leap occurs from a degree to one not adjacent. And it may also be by properties and accidents. So knowledge of the five states is beneficial in syllogisms, and its benefit in definitions and descriptions is more apparent; for definitions are from genera and differentiae, and descriptions are from genera and properties. So presenting knowledge of these states that attach to single terms before starting in knowledge of compound terms is either necessary or nearly necessary. And single terms have other states, namely their signification of existing things, and there is no necessity to know those states when learning the craft of logic, neither from the aspect of their signification of particular individuals, for that is not beneficial in any science at all, let alone logic. Nor from the aspect of their signification of species, for the craft of logic is apart from that. And it suffices without it. Nor from the aspect of their signification of the highest genera called the categories. For the learner of logic, when he transitions after knowledge of single terms and nouns and words, it is possible for him to transition to learning propositions and their divisions, and syllogisms and definitions and their varieties, and demonstrative and non-demonstrative definitions, their genera and species. Even if it does not occur to him that there are ten categories or not ten, no significant defect arises from that. And that these are the things that are necessarily described as generic, and other things described as specific. Rather, knowledge of these: either from the aspect of the modality of existence, it belongs to first philosophy; or from the aspect of having a concept itself, it belongs to a part of natural science adjacent to first philosophy; and knowledge that they deserve words placed corresponding to them as primary or secondary position belongs to the craft of language. And it is not that the logician qua logician knows that more than other knowledge. Yes, there is one thing: the learner may benefit from this in a certain way.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological argument about the scope of logic. The author explains that while knowledge of the five universals (الأحوال الخمسة) is necessary or nearly necessary for learning logic, knowledge of the ten categories (المقولات العشر) is not. He begins by stating that the purpose of discussing categories is to clarify that there are other states of single terms that are not needed for compound terms. The compound terms in logic are composed according to the craft of logic and depend on beneficial paths in assent (تصديق) and conception (تصور). These are completed by benefits (al-fa'idat, i.e., beneficial propositions), definitions, descriptions, and syllogisms. Premises must have universal subjects to be part of sciences, and their subjects and predicates must be related essentially or accidentally to enter demonstration (برهان). Division (قسمة) also leads to knowledge, preserving order without leaps. Knowledge of the five universals is beneficial for syllogisms and more so for definitions and descriptions. Thus, learning these states is necessary or nearly necessary. The other states of single terms — their signification of particulars, species, and highest genera (the categories) — are not necessary for logic. A learner can proceed to propositions, syllogisms, and definitions without ever thinking of the categories. Knowledge of categories belongs to first philosophy ( metaphysics), natural science, or linguistics. The logician as logician does not need to prioritize them. However, there is one small benefit: a learner may gain some advantage from knowing categories. This passage refines the scope of logical study, warning against overloading the beginner with unnecessary ontology.

Defining Substance and Accident: The Categories of Existence
Author defines substance and accident with careful distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define substance and accident according to this passage, and distinguish between matter, locus, subject, form, and accident.

Core Idea
Existence is either necessary or possible; possible is either substance or accident.

In this passage, the author sets the stage by dividing all existents into the necessary (the Creator) and the possible (all others). Possible things are then split into two fundamental categories: substance, which exists not in a subject, and accident, which exists in a subject. But the author goes deeper, clarifying the relationships between matter, form, locus, and subject. Understanding these distinctions is essential for any student of kalam or falsafa, as they form the building blocks of arguments for God's existence and the nature of the cosmos.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish necessary and possible existence
Identify the author's first division: that which does not preclude existence is either necessary in itself (the Creator) or possible in itself (everything else). This is the starting point for all subsequent classification.
The necessary exists without dependence; the possible depends on a cause.
2
Identify the two types of possible existents
Among possible things, there are two and only two kinds: those that exist not in a subject (substance) and those that exist in a subject (accident). This is the core definition given here.
The subject (mawdu‘) is carefully distinguished from matter and locus.
3
Understand the fourfold relation: matter/form and subject/accident
When one thing inheres in another, two possibilities arise: either the inhering thing causes the locus to exist (form-matter relation) or the locus causes the inhering thing to exist (subject-accident relation). This yields four technical terms: matter (madda), form (sura), subject (mawdu‘), and accident (‘arad).
The author stresses that 'subject' is more specific than 'locus' (mahall); matter and subject share the same generality under locus, while form and accident share under the inhering thing (hal).
4
Classify given entities into substance or accident using the definitions
Practice applying the definitions: decide for an entity whether it exists in a subject or not, and if it is a substance, identify which of the five types it fits (hyle, form, body, soul, intellect).
This step consolidates the lesson's learning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الجوهر والعرض فإنهما من جملة ما لا يمتنع وجوده، وما لا يمتنع وجوده من الأشياء فذلك إما أن يكون واجبًا لذاته وهو الذي لا يفتقر في وجوده إلى الغير كالصانع تعالى وتقدس، وإما أن لا يكون؛ بل يكون ممكناً لذاته وهو الذي يفتقر كالمصنوعات. وإنه أي الممكن لا يخلو من أن يكون موجودًا لا في موضوع وهو الجوهر، أو موجودًا في موضوع وهو العرض، والموضوع هنا غير المادة وغير المحل لا محالة فإنه إذا حل شيء في شيء فإما أن يكون الحال سببًا لوجود المحل، وحينئذ يسمى الحال صورة والمحل مادة. والحلول عبارة عن اختصاص أحد الشيئين بالآخر على وجه يكون الإشارة إلى أحدهما عين الإشارة إلى الآخر. وإما أن يكون بالعكس كما إذا كان المحل سببًا لوجود الحال، وحينئذ يسمى المحل موضوعا والحال عرضًا. فالموضوع أخص من المحل فيكون نقيضه أعم من بعض ذلك، فالمادة والموضوع إذن يشتركان اشتراك الأخصين تحت الأعم وهو المحل، والصورة والعرض يشتركان اشتراك الأخصين تحت الأعم وهو الحال. ثم الجوهر إما أن يكون محلا وهو الهيولى، أو حالا وهو الصورة، أو مركبا منهما وهو الجسم، أو لا حالا ولا محلا ولا مركبا منهما؛ وذلك إما أن يكون متعلقا بالجسم تعلق التدبير وهو النفس، أو لا يكون وهو العقل. والجوهر عند الجمهور لا يكون جنسًا لهذه الخمسة؛ إذ الماهية التي يقال عليها الجوهر إن كانت بسيطة لم يكن الجوهر جنسًا وإلا لكانت تلك الماهية مركبة من الجنس والفصل؛ وإن كانت مركبة فبسائطها إن كانت أعراضًا كان الجوهر متقوما بالأعراض؛ وإن كانت جواهر وهي لبساطتها غير مندرجة
As for substance and accident, they are among what does not preclude existence. That which does not preclude existence from things is either necessary in itself, which is that which does not depend for its existence on another, like the Creator, exalted and sanctified, or it is not, rather it is possible in itself, which depends, like created things. And that which is possible is not free from either being existent not in a subject, which is substance, or being existent in a subject, which is accident. The subject here is other than matter and other than locus inevitably. For when something inheres in something, either the inhering thing is a cause for the existence of the locus, and then the inhering thing is called form and the locus matter. And inherence is an expression of the specification of one of the two things by the other in such a way that the indication of one is identical to the indication of the other. Or it may be the opposite, as when the locus is a cause for the existence of the inhering thing, and then the locus is called subject and the inhering thing accident. So the subject is more specific than the locus, and thus its opposite is more general than some of that. Matter and subject then share the sharing of the more specific under the more general, which is the locus, and form and accident share the sharing of the more specific under the more general, which is the inhering thing. Then substance is either a locus, which is hyle, or an inhering thing, which is form, or composite of both, which is body, or neither a locus nor an inhering thing nor composite of both; that is either related to body by way of management, which is soul, or not, which is intellect. And substance according to the majority is not a genus for these five; for if the quiddity which is called substance is simple, then substance would not be a genus, otherwise that quiddity would be composed of genus and differentia; and if it is composite, then if its simples are accidents, then substance would be constituted by accidents; and if they are substances, due to their simplicity they are not subsumed
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory explanation of substance (jawhar) and accident (‘arad). It begins by distinguishing necessary existence (the Creator) from possible existence (creatures). Possible existents are then divided into what exists not in a subject (substance) and what exists in a subject (accident). The author carefully distinguishes ‘subject’ (mawdu‘) from ‘matter’ (madda) and ‘locus’ (mahall). He clarifies that inherence (hulul) can be of two types: either the inhering thing (hal) causes the existence of the locus, then it is called form and the locus matter; or conversely, the locus causes the existence of the inhering thing, then the locus is called subject and the inhering thing accident. Thus subject is more specific than locus, and matter and subject both fall under locus, while form and accident fall under the inhering thing. Then five types of substance are listed: (1) hyle (prime matter) which is a locus, (2) form which is an inhering thing, (3) body which is composite of both, (4) soul which manages the body, and (5) intellect which does not manage. Finally, the author argues that substance is not a true genus for these five. If the quiddity called substance is simple, then substance cannot be a genus (since a genus must be more general and composite with differentia). If it is composite, then its simple components would be either accidents (then substance would be constituted by accidents, which is impossible) or substances (then they would be simple and not subsumed under a higher genus, so substance is not a genus). The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clear: the five substances do not share a common genus.

Key Takeaway
The division of possible existents into substance and accident, along with the careful distinction of subject, matter, locus, form, and accident, is the foundation for all further metaphysical analysis in the Islamic tradition.

Why Substance is Not a Genus: The Five Substances
Author lists five substances and argues substance is not a genus over them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the argument that substance is not a genus for the five types, and identify the logical dilemma the author uses.

Core Idea
The five substances do not share a common genus because their quiddity is either simple or composite, leading to a contradiction.

After defining substance and accident, the author lists the five kinds of substance: hyle (prime matter), form, body, soul, and intellect. He then raises a critical question: is 'substance' a genus that encompasses all these five? The answer, according to the majority (al-jumhur), is no. In this lesson we will unpack the author's concise argument, which uses a case analysis of the quiddity of substance to show why it cannot be a true genus. This is a classic example of logical reasoning applied to metaphysical categories.

Guided Path
1
List the five substances
Memorise the five: (1) hyle – a locus (mahall) only, (2) form – an inhering thing (hal) only, (3) body – composite of both, (4) soul – related to body by management, (5) intellect – neither managing nor composite.
These are the Aristotelian categories of substance.
2
Recall what a genus is
A genus is a universal that is predicated of many species differing in kind, and it enters into the definition of the species as a part (e.g., 'animal' is the genus of 'human'). A genus must be more general than its species and must be combined with a differentia.
If substance were a genus, each of the five would be species that share a common definition of 'substance' plus a specific difference.
3
Analyse the first case: the quiddity of substance is simple
Assume that the quiddity (mahiyya) that we call 'substance' is simple (i.e., not composed of genus and differentia). In that case, it cannot serve as a genus because a genus itself must be composed with a differentia to form a species. A simple universal cannot be a genus.
This is a metaphysical claim: a genus must have internal composition to be divided by differentiae.
4
Analyse the second case: the quiddity is composite
Now suppose the quiddity of substance is composite, made of simpler components. Those simpler components must be either accidents or other substances. If they are accidents, then substance would be constituted by accidents, which is absurd because substance is prior to accidents. If they are other substances, then those substances are simple (and not composite) and thus cannot be further resolved, meaning they themselves are not species of a higher genus. In either subcase, substance cannot be a true genus.
The author's argument is a dilemma: both branches lead to the conclusion that substance is not a genus.
5
Conclude that substance is not a genus
Since both possible cases (quiddity simple or composite) lead to contradiction or impossibility, the only coherent conclusion is that substance is not a genus covering these five types. They are not species of a single genus; rather, they are grouped together by analogy or some other relation.
This conclusion is held by the majority (al-jumhur).
