As for substance and accident, they are among what does not preclude existence. That which does not preclude existence from things is either necessary in itself, which is that which does not depend for its existence on another, like the Creator, exalted and sanctified, or it is not, rather it is possible in itself, which depends, like created things. And that which is possible is not free from either being existent not in a subject, which is substance, or being existent in a subject, which is accident. The subject here is other than matter and other than locus inevitably. For when something inheres in something, either the inhering thing is a cause for the existence of the locus, and then the inhering thing is called form and the locus matter. And inherence is an expression of the specification of one of the two things by the other in such a way that the indication of one is identical to the indication of the other. Or it may be the opposite, as when the locus is a cause for the existence of the inhering thing, and then the locus is called subject and the inhering thing accident. So the subject is more specific than the locus, and thus its opposite is more general than some of that. Matter and subject then share the sharing of the more specific under the more general, which is the locus, and form and accident share the sharing of the more specific under the more general, which is the inhering thing. Then substance is either a locus, which is hyle, or an inhering thing, which is form, or composite of both, which is body, or neither a locus nor an inhering thing nor composite of both; that is either related to body by way of management, which is soul, or not, which is intellect. And substance according to the majority is not a genus for these five; for if the quiddity which is called substance is simple, then substance would not be a genus, otherwise that quiddity would be composed of genus and differentia; and if it is composite, then if its simples are accidents, then substance would be constituted by accidents; and if they are substances, due to their simplicity they are not subsumed
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory explanation of substance (jawhar) and accident (‘arad). It begins by distinguishing necessary existence (the Creator) from possible existence (creatures). Possible existents are then divided into what exists not in a subject (substance) and what exists in a subject (accident). The author carefully distinguishes ‘subject’ (mawdu‘) from ‘matter’ (madda) and ‘locus’ (mahall). He clarifies that inherence (hulul) can be of two types: either the inhering thing (hal) causes the existence of the locus, then it is called form and the locus matter; or conversely, the locus causes the existence of the inhering thing, then the locus is called subject and the inhering thing accident. Thus subject is more specific than locus, and matter and subject both fall under locus, while form and accident fall under the inhering thing. Then five types of substance are listed: (1) hyle (prime matter) which is a locus, (2) form which is an inhering thing, (3) body which is composite of both, (4) soul which manages the body, and (5) intellect which does not manage. Finally, the author argues that substance is not a true genus for these five. If the quiddity called substance is simple, then substance cannot be a genus (since a genus must be more general and composite with differentia). If it is composite, then its simple components would be either accidents (then substance would be constituted by accidents, which is impossible) or substances (then they would be simple and not subsumed under a higher genus, so substance is not a genus). The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clear: the five substances do not share a common genus.

جنس

جسم
نفس
عقل
ماهية

Checkpoint

B) The quiddity is simple, so it cannot be a genus.
C) The five substances are all bodies.
D) The author says substance is a genus but the majority disagrees.

Key Takeaway
Substance is not a true genus for hyle, form, body, soul, and intellect; the term is applied to them by analogy, not by a shared essence.

?

The Two Modes of Existence: Necessary and Possible
Classification of existence into necessary and possible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any existent as either necessary by itself or possible by itself based on its dependency on others.

Core Idea
Existence is either self-subsistent or dependent; the Creator is necessary, creation is possible.

This passage grounds substance and accident in a broader classification of existence. Before we define substance and accident, the author reminds us that everything that exists falls into two fundamental categories: that which requires nothing else for its existence, and that which does. This distinction is the metaphysical foundation of Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of existence
Find the two divisions: necessary by itself and possible by itself. Note the defining characteristic of each.
Necessary: does not need others. Possible: needs others.
2
Recognize the examples
The author gives the Creator as necessary and created things as possible. Why is this significant? In Islamic thought, only God is necessary; everything else is possible.
3
Apply the classification
Given an entity, determine whether it is necessary or possible based on its dependency. Note that necessity is reserved for God alone in this context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الجوهر والعرض فإنهما من جملة ما لا يمتنع وجوده، وما لا يمتنع وجوده من الأشياء فذلك إما أن يكون واجبًا لذاته وهو الذي لا يفتقر في وجوده إلى الغير كالصانع تعالى وتقدس، وإما أن لا يكون؛ بل يكون ممكناً لذاته وهو الذي يفتقر كالمصنوعات . وإنه أي الممكن لا يخلو من أن يكون موجودًا لا في موضوع وهو الجوهر، أو موجودًا في موضوع وهو العرض، والموضوع هنا غير المادة وغير المحل لا محالة فإنه إذا حل شيء في شيء فإما أن يكون الحال سببًا لوجود المحل، وحينئذ يسمى الحال صورة والمحل مادة. والحلول عبارة عن اختصاص أحد الشيئين بالآخر على وجه يكون الإشارة إلى أحدهما عين الإشارة إلى الآخر. وإما أن يكون بالعكس كما إذا كان المحل سببًا لوجود الحال، وحينئذ يسمى المحل موضوعا والحال عرضًا. فالموضوع أخص من المحل فيكون نقيضه أعم من بعض ذلك، فالمادة والموضوع إذن يشتركان اشتراك الأخصين تحت الأعم وهو المحل، والصورة والعرض يشتركان اشتراك الأخصين تحت الأعم وهو الحال.
As for substance and accident, they are among that whose existence is not impossible. And that whose existence is not impossible from things is either necessary by itself, which is that which does not need others in its existence, like the Creator, exalted and sanctified; or it is not, rather it is possible by itself, which is that which needs others, like created things. And this possible is either existent not in a subject, which is substance, or existent in a subject, which is accident. The subject here is other than matter and other than locus, necessarily. For when something resides in something, either the resident is the cause of the existence of the locus, and then the resident is called form and the locus matter. And 'residence' (hulul) is an expression for the specification of one of the two things by the other in such a way that pointing to one is identical to pointing to the other. Or the opposite, as when the locus is the cause of the existence of the resident, and then the locus is called subject and the resident accident. The subject is more specific than the locus, so its opposite is more general than some of that. Matter and subject, then, share in the manner of two specific things under a more general thing, which is the locus; and form and accident share in the manner of two specific things under a more general thing, which is the resident.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of substance (jawhar) and accident ('arad). The author begins by grounding the discussion in the broader classification of existence: everything whose existence is not impossible is either necessary by itself (wajib li-dhatih) or possible by itself (mumkin li-dhatih). The necessary is independent of others; the possible depends on others. Within the possible, a further division is made: either it exists not in a subject (substance) or in a subject (accident). Crucially, the author distinguishes the term 'subject' (mawdu') from 'matter' (madda) and 'locus' (mahall). The explanation uses the concept of 'residing' (hulul): when one thing resides in another, if the resident is the cause of the existence of the locus, the resident is called form (sura) and the locus matter (madda). If the locus is the cause of existence of the resident, then the locus is called subject and the resident accident. This yields a hierarchy: subject is a subset of locus, and matter is another subset. Matter and subject both fall under locus; form and accident both fall under resident (hal). This precise terminology clarifies that 'subject' in the definition of accident is not just any locus, but specifically the thing that causes the accident's existence (as in the accident's existence depends on the subject), unlike matter which is caused by the form. The passage sets up the terminological distinctions necessary for the subsequent discussion of the five substances and the nature of accident. Examples from the Islamic sciences would be: the Creator as necessary existent; created entities as possible. In fiqh, the attribute of prohibition (haram) is an accident residing in an act (subject) because the act's existence supports the prohibition, not the other way around.

Key Terms
واجب لذاته
ممكن لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The universe
B) A tree
C) The Creator
D) A human soul

Key Takeaway
Existence is divided into necessary by itself (independent) and possible by itself (dependent); this classification underpins all further ontological analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the concept of 'human being'. Is it necessary or possible by itself? Explain your reasoning using the criteria from the passage.

7 min
Defining Substance and Accident
Definition of substance and accident within possible existents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define substance and accident in terms of existence 'in a subject' and identify the key difference.

Core Idea
Substance exists independently of a subject; accident exists in a subject and depends on it.

After establishing the necessary/possible division, the author narrows the focus to possible existents. Within the possible, something either exists not in a subject—that is substance—or exists in a subject—that is accident. This is the classical Aristotelian definition adopted in Islamic logic. The critical term 'subject' (mawdu') will be refined in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Isolate the possible existents
Remember that substance and accident are both possible, not necessary. Why is that important? Because necessary existence is only for God; creatures are possible.
2
Extract the definition of substance
Find the phrase: 'existent not in a subject'. What does 'not in a subject' mean? It means substance's existence does not depend on being in something else.
3
Extract the definition of accident
Find the phrase: 'existent in a subject'. What does 'in a subject' imply about dependency? It implies the accident's existence depends on the subject.
4
Contrast the two definitions
How does the notion of 'subject' create the distinction? Substance does not need a subject; accident does.

Source Text

10 min
The Precise Meaning of 'Subject' in Accident: Subject vs. Matter vs. Locus
Distinction between subject, matter, and locus based on causality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between subject (mawdu'), matter (madda), and locus (mahall) based on the causal relation of existence.

Core Idea
Not every locus is a subject; the subject is the cause of the accident's existence, while matter is caused by the form.

The definition of accident as 'existent in a subject' uses 'subject' in a specific technical sense. The author now clarifies that 'subject' is not just any place (locus) where something resides. In the process of hulul (residing), when the resident (hal) is the cause of the locus's existence, it is called form and the locus is matter. When the locus is the cause of the resident's existence, the locus is called subject and the resident is accident. This causal asymmetry is crucial.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of hulul (residing)
The passage defines hulul as one thing being specified with another so that pointing to one is pointing to the other. This is a technical relation indicating that the two things are spatially or ontologically joined.
2
Identify the two causal directions
Find the two scenarios: resident causes locus (form-matter), or locus causes resident (subject-accident).
3
Map the terms correctly
When the cause is the resident → resident = form, locus = matter. When the cause is the locus → locus = subject, resident = accident.
4
Compare the terms
Notice that 'subject' is a subset of 'locus' (specifically when the locus is the cause), and 'matter' is another subset. Similarly, 'form' and 'accident' are subsets of 'resident'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

10 min
Distinguishing the Accident-Subject Relation from Other Modes of Inherence
Author distinguishes existence in a subject (accident) from other types of existence in another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the technical 'existence in a subject' (accident) from other types of inherence by identifying the condition of inseparable dependence.

Core Idea
An accident's existence is uniquely dependent on its subject; no other mode of inherence shares that dependence.

In the previous lesson, you learned that an accident is defined as 'that which exists in a subject.' But what exactly does 'existing in a subject' mean? This passage clarifies by contrasting it with several other ways a thing can exist in another, such as form in matter or a part in a whole. Understanding this distinction is critical for applying the category of accident in Islamic logic and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target relation
The passage focuses on the accident-subject relation. Locate the description of how an accident relates to its subject: it cannot leave its subject without being destroyed.
2
Examine each counterexample
For each example (form in matter, part in whole, genus in species, species in genus, place/time/state), note why it fails to be a true 'subject' relation. The common thread is that the 'host' is not a subject because it depends on what it contains.
3
Extract the key condition
What condition must hold for something to be a true subject? The passage says: the accident cannot leave its particular subject without its subsistence being nullified, and its subsistence is by that subject.
4
Apply the condition
Test one counterexample: why does form in matter not meet the condition? Because matter is constituted by form, not the other way around. Matter depends on form for its existence, so it cannot be a subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعرض غير الجوهر بالضرورة، وإنه هو الموجود في الموضوع كما مر . والشیء قد يكون موجودًا في الموضوع وقد لا يكون؛ بل يكون موجودًا في الغير، وذلك مثل صورة الماء في المادة القابلة لها، والمادة لا تكون موضوعة لتلك الصورة، وإنها أي المادة لا تكون متقومة الذات إلا بالصورة المائية. ومثل وجود الجزء في الكل كالإحساس في الحيوان والكل لا يمكن أن يكون متقوما إلا بالجزء فلا يمكن أن يكون موضوعا له كذلك. ومثل الجنس في النوع كالحيوان في الإنسان، فإن طبيعة النوع متقومة لطبيعة الجنس، والمتقوم بالغير لا يكون موضوعًا لذلك الغير. ومثل النوع في الجنس كالإنسان في الحيوان الذي هو أعم منه فإن عموم الجنس متقوم بالنوع فالجنس لا يكون جنسًا إلا وأن يكون شاملا للنوعين فصاعدًا، أو ما يكون كذلك، فذلك لا يكون موضوعا. ومثل كون الشيء في المكان أو في الزمان أو في حالة واحدة مثل ما يكون في الصحة أو في المرض مثلا. فإن الجسم في المكان لا يكون متقوما بالمكان وإنه قد يفارق مكانه إلى غيره ولا يبطل قوامه، وكذلك ما يكون في الزمان فإنه يفارق الزمان وهو على قوامه. وعلى هذا في الذي يكون في الصحة والمرض فإنه يفارق الصحة والمرض أيضًا، وإنه على قوامه كذلك، ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن أن يكون واحد منها موضوعًا لما هو فيه. وأما الموجود في الموضوع وهو العرض في الجوهر الذي هو فيه فذلك على خلاف هذا كله لما أنه لا يفارق موضوعه بعينه، وقد كان قوامه بذلك
And accident is other than substance necessarily, and it is that which exists in a subject, as has passed. And a thing may exist in a subject or may not; rather it exists in another. That is like the form of water in the matter receptive to it; the matter is not a subject for that form, and the matter is not self-subsistent except by the watery form. And like the existence of a part in the whole, such as sensation in animal; the whole cannot be subsistent except by the part, so it cannot be a subject for it likewise. And like the genus in the species, as animal in man; for the nature of the species is constitutive of the nature of the genus, and that which is constituted by another is not a subject for that other. And like the species in the genus, as man in animal which is more general than it; for the generality of the genus is constituted by the species, so the genus is not a genus unless it encompasses two or more species, or what is like that, so that is not a subject. And like a thing being in place or in time, or in a single state, such as being in health or illness for example. For the body in place is not constituted by place, and it may leave its place to another without its subsistence being nullified; likewise what is in time, for it leaves time while it is in its subsistence. And similarly for what is in health and illness, for it leaves health and illness as well while it is in its subsistence. And if it is so, then none of them can be a subject for what it is in. As for that which exists in a subject, and it is the accident in the substance which it is in, that is contrary to all this, because it does not leave its particular subject, and its subsistence is by that.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage clarifies the precise meaning of 'existing in a subject' as the defining characteristic of accident. The author contrasts it with several other ways a thing can 'exist in' another, none of which qualify as 'subject' in the technical sense. The key point is that an accident cannot exist independently of its subject; separation from its subject would destroy it. The examples: (1) Form in matter: the form exists in matter, but matter is not a subject; rather, matter's existence depends on form. (2) Part in whole: a part exists in a whole, but the whole depends on the part. (3) Genus in species: the genus 'animal' exists in the species 'man', but the species constitutes the genus, not vice versa. (4) Species in genus: 'man' exists in more general 'animal', but the genus's generality depends on having species, so it is not a subject. (5) Place, time, state: a body exists in a place, but leaving the place does not destroy the body, so place is not a subject. The subject as defined earlier is something that causes the accident's existence and cannot be separated from it. This distinction is crucial in usul al-fiqh for example: a ruling (hukm) like 'obligation' is an accident attached to an act; the act is the subject, and the obligation cannot exist without that act. If the act is not prescribed, the obligation vanishes.

Key Terms
الموضوع
المادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because form and matter are inseparable.
D) Because form exists in time, not in a subject.

Key Takeaway
The accident-subject relation is unique because the accident cannot exist without its particular subject, unlike other forms of inherence such as form in matter or part in whole.

When is a Thing Truly 'In' a Subject? Substance and Accident
Author distinguishes substance and accident, explaining that only accident truly exists in a subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish cases of genuine 'existence in a subject' (accidents) from other types of inherence such as part-whole, genus-species, and spatiotemporal location.

Core Idea
Only accidents truly inhere in a subject; other relations are mere dependency.

The author defines accident as that which exists in a subject, but not every relation of 'being in another' qualifies. To avoid confusion, he enumerates five other types of inherence and explains why they are not true subjecthood. This lesson walks through the criteria that make accident unique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of accident
Find the phrase 'al-mawjud fi al-mawdu' (the existent in a subject). This is the defining property of accident.
2
Contrast with substance
Note that substance (jawhar) is defined negatively: it does not exist in a subject and is self-subsistent.
3
Examine the five other types of inherence
The author lists: form in matter, part in whole, genus in species, species in genus, and thing in place/time/state. For each, ask whether the supposed 'subject' is constituted by what is in it.
4
Test each type against the criteria
In each case, the 'subject' depends on the inmate (e.g., matter is constituted by form, whole depends on parts). Thus it cannot be a true subject because it is not independent.
5
Conclude why accident is special
Accident cannot leave its specific subject without ceasing to exist. Its very essence depends on that subject. This inseparability is lacking in the other cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحت الجنس فالجوهر ليس بجنس، ولا يمكن أن يكون البعض من تلك البسائط جوهرًا والبعض منها عرضًا. والجوهر من حيث هو الجوهر لا يمكن أن يكون متقوما بالعرض والجوهر مع العرض لا يكون جوهرا ولا يكون عرضًا كذلك. وإن كان العرض قائما به؛ بل يكون المجموع جوهرًا وعرضًا، والعرض غير الجوهر بالضرورة، وإنه هو الموجود في الموضوع كما مر. والشيء قد يكون موجودًا في الموضوع وقد لا يكون؛ بل يكون موجودًا في الغير، وذلك مثل صورة الماء في المادة القابلة لها، والمادة لا تكون موضوعة لتلك الصورة، وإنها أي المادة لا تكون متقومة الذات إلا بالصورة المائية. ومثل وجود الجزء في الكل كالإحساس في الحيوان والكل لا يمكن أن يكون متقوما إلا بالجزء فلا يمكن أن يكون موضوعا له كذلك. ومثل الجنس في النوع كالحيوان في الإنسان، فإن طبيعة النوع متقومة لطبيعة الجنس، والمتقوم بالغير لا يكون موضوعًا لذلك الغير. ومثل النوع في الجنس كالإنسان في الحيوان الذي هو أعم منه فإن عموم الجنس متقوم بالنوع فالجنس لا يكون جنسًا إلا وأن يكون شاملا للنوعين فصاعدًا، أو ما يكون كذلك، فذلك لا يكون موضوعا. ومثل كون الشيء في المكان أو في الزمان أو في حالة واحدة مثل ما يكون في الصحة أو في المرض مثلا. فإن الجسم في المكان لا يكون متقوما بالمكان وإنه قد يفارق مكانه إلى غيره ولا يبطل قوامه، وكذلك ما يكون في الزمان فإنه يفارق الزمان وهو على قوامه. وعلى هذا في الذي يكون في الصحة والمرض فإنه يفارق الصحة والمرض أيضًا، وإنه على قوامه كذلك، ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن أن يكون واحد منها موضوعًا لما هو فيه. وأما الموجود في الموضوع وهو العرض في الجوهر الذي هو فيه فذلك على خلاف هذا كله لما أنه لا يفارق موضوعه بعينه، وقد كان قوامه بذلك الموضوع لا لأمر آخر سوى ذلك الموضوع على ما عرف.
Under genus: substance is not a genus, and it is impossible that some of those simples be substance and some accident. Substance as such cannot be constituted by accident, and substance with accident is not substance nor accident, even if accident subsists in it; rather the composite is substance and accident. Accident is necessarily other than substance, and it is that which exists in a subject as previously mentioned. A thing may exist in a subject or not; rather it may exist in another, such as the form of water in the matter receptive to it, and matter is not a subject for that form, and matter is not self-subsistent except through the watery form. And like the existence of a part in the whole, as sensation in animal; the whole cannot be constituted except by the part, so it cannot be a subject for it. And like genus in species, as animal in man; for the nature of species is constitutive of the nature of genus, and that which is constituted by another is not a subject for that other. And like species in genus, as man in animal which is more general than it; for the universality of genus is constituted by species, so genus is not a genus unless it encompasses two or more species, or what is like that; so that is not a subject. And like a thing being in place or in time or in a single state, such as in health or illness. The body in place is not constituted by place, and it may leave its place for another without its constitution being nullified. Likewise what is in time, it leaves time while its constitution remains. And similarly what is in health and illness, it leaves health and illness as well while its constitution remains. And if that is the case, then none of these can be a subject for what it is in. As for what exists in a subject, which is accident in the substance it is in, that is contrary to all this because it does not leave its specific subject, and its constitution is by that subject, not by something else other than that subject, as has been known.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage defines the philosophical distinction between substance (jawhar) and accident (arad) by focusing on the unique mode of inherence called 'existing in a subject' (al-mawjud fi al-mawdu). The author begins by clarifying that substance cannot be a genus in the same way as other genera, and that the combination of substance and accident yields a composite that is neither purely substance nor purely accident. The core of the discussion is that accident alone truly 'exists in a subject'—meaning it cannot exist without that specific subject. The author then enumerates several other types of 'existence in another' that are not true existence in a subject: the form of water in matter (where matter is constituted by the form and thus is not a subject), the part in the whole (the whole depends on the part), genus in species and species in genus (each is constitutive of the other), and the existence of a thing in place, time, or state (all are separable from the thing without destroying its essence). In each case, the thing 'in' something can be separated, unlike accident which is inseparable from its subject. The key point is that accident's entire essence depends on the particular subject it inheres in, which is not true for the other cases.

Key Takeaway
Accident is defined by its inseparable existence in a subject, a property not shared by other types of inherence like part-whole, genus-species, or spatiotemporal location.

Refuting Whole-Part Inherence
Refutation of whole-part inherence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the claim that the whole exists in its parts is invalid and distinguish it from accident in a subject.

Core Idea
The whole is its parts, not something in them.

In logic, understanding how entities relate to what they are in is crucial. One common error is to think that a whole exists in its parts like an accident in a subject. The author refutes this by showing that whole and parts are not distinct, so the inherence relation cannot apply.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Find the statement that the author is refuting: 'the whole exists in its parts' (وجود الكل في الأجزاء). This is proposed by some as a way to distinguish the whole from an accident in a subject.
2
Understand the condition for inherence
The author states that for something to exist in another, they must be distinct: 'this is other than that and vice versa' (هذا غير ذلك وبالعكس). This is a necessary condition for the relation of 'being in'.
3
Apply the condition to whole and parts
The whole is defined as the sum of its parts (مجموع الأجزاء). Therefore, the whole is not distinct from its parts; it is identical to them collectively. So the condition for inherence is not met.
The author gives the example of three and its units: three is those units, not something separate.
4
Recognize the absurd consequence
If the whole were in the parts, it would be like a thing being in itself (نسبة الشيء إلى نفسه بأنه فيها), which is impossible. Thus the claim is false, and the distinction between accident and subject remains intact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الشيء لا يكون موجودًا في شيء إلا وأن يكون هذا غير ذلك وبالعكس. ولا يلتفت إلى قول من يقول إن وجود الكل في الأجزاء طلبًا للفرق بينه وبين العرض في الموضوع إذ هو باطل، وقد كان الكل عبارة عن مجموع الأجزاء. فلا يقال إن الكل في الأجزاء؛ بل يقال إن الكل هو الأجزاء إلا فردًا فردًا بل جملة. ولو كان كذلك فالكل لا ينسب إلى فرد فرد؛ وإنه لا يكون في هذا الفرد، ولا في ذلك كذلك هلم جرا؛ بل ينسب إلى جملتها، وذلك لا يمكن وهو جملة الأجزاء فتكون نسبته إليها كنسبة الشيء إلى نفسه بأنه فيها، وذلك باطل يعرف على التحقيق من بعد. فلا يقال الثلاثة في آحادها مثلا، والثلاثة هي تلك الآحاد، وبهذا ظهر الفرق بين العرض وبين غيره في الموضوع.
Then a thing does not exist in something unless this is other than that, and vice versa. And no attention should be paid to the saying of whoever says that the whole exists in the parts, seeking a distinction between it and accident in the subject, for it is false. The whole is an expression for the sum of the parts. So it is not said that the whole is in the parts; rather it is said that the whole is the parts, not individually but collectively. And if that is the case, the whole is not attributed to individual parts; it is not in this part nor in that, and so on; rather it is attributed to their totality, and that is not possible while it is the totality of the parts, so its relation to them is like the relation of a thing to itself, that it is in it, and that is false, as will be known upon investigation later. So it is not said, for example, that three is in its units, while three is those units. And by this the distinction between accident and other than it in the subject becomes clear.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a mistaken view: that the whole exists in its parts in a way analogous to an accident existing in its subject. The author argues that this is false because for one thing to truly exist in another, the two must be distinct (this is other than that and vice versa). But the whole is not something other than its parts; it is precisely the sum of the parts. You cannot say 'the whole is in the parts' any more than you can say 'three is in its units'—three is those units. Therefore, the relation between whole and parts is one of identity, not inherence. This refutation clarifies the unique nature of accident in subject, where the accident is genuinely distinct from and dependent on the subject.

الأجزاء

Key Takeaway
For something to exist in another, they must be distinct; the whole and its parts are identical, so the whole cannot exist in the parts.

The Fourfold Classification of Entities
Author classifies entities into four types based on predication and inherence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given entity into one of the four logical categories using the criteria of predication and inherence.

Core Idea
Everything in logic fits into four boxes based on two questions: Is it predicated of many? Does it exist in a subject?

In this passage, the author establishes a fundamental fourfold division of all entities—whether concepts or real things—based on two simple criteria: whether they are 'said of many' (universal) and whether they 'exist in a subject' (accident vs substance). This classification is the backbone of logical definitions and demonstrations, providing a clear map that every student of logic must internalize. Let us walk through the criteria and the four resulting categories with examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two criteria
The first criterion is 'predicated of a subject' (maqūla ʿalā mawḍūʿ): can the term be asserted of many individuals? The second is 'existing in a subject' (mawjūd fī mawḍūʿ): does the entity inhere in another as a substrate? Together they yield a 2×2 grid.
2
Identify the four combinations
List all combinations: (a) predicated, not existing; (b) existing, not predicated; (c) both predicated and existing; (d) neither. The author labels these as universal substances, particular accidents, universal accidents, and particular substances respectively.
3
Map the author's examples
Substance universals: e.g., the genus 'animal' is predicated of Zayd but does not exist in a subject. Accident particulars: a specific whiteness exists in a wall but is not said of many. Accident universals: 'whiteness' itself is said of many whitenesses and exists in a subject. Substance particulars: Zayd himself is neither predicated of others nor exists in a subject.
The passage also mentions 'this matter and form' and 'this soul' as examples of particular substances.
4
Apply to new examples from Islamic sciences
Take a concept like 'justice' (ʿadl) as a universal accident; it is predicated of many just actions and exists in the agent as a quality. Or consider the species 'human' as a universal substance: predicated of individuals but not an accident.
This practice solidifies the logical categories for use in kalam and fiqh reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشاركة أن يكون ما هو مفهوم للاسم حدا أو رسما يحمل على الشيء الذي يحمل عليه الاسم فيوصف الشيء بمعنى الاسم كما يسمى بلفظه . وإن لم يكن ذلك حدا له بهذه الأشياء تبين أنهم أغفلوا إغفالا كثيرًا؛ بل قد يتبين لك أن كل معنى عام يقال على أكثر من واحد فهو كلي والمعنى الخاص جزئي، وأن العرض الذي يقال الجوهر هو الذي مرَّ ذكره، وأن الأمور إما مقولة على موضوع غير موجود في موضوع وهي الكليات التي هي جواهر، فلأنها كلية تقال على [موضوع]، ولأنها جواهر فلا توجد في [موضوع]؛ وإما موجود في موضوع غير مقولة على موضوع وهي جزئيات الأعراض. فإنها لا بها أعراض موجودة في، ولأنها جزئية ليست على. وأما مقولة على موضوع موجود في موضوع وهي كليات الأعراض فإنها بالقياس إلى جزئياتها كالبياض الكلي بالقياس إلى بياض ما مقولة على موضوع؛ ولأنها أعراض فهي موجودة في موضوع. وأما لا مقولة على [موضوع] ولا موجودة في[موضوع] وهي جزئيات الجواهر كزيد وعمرو، وهذه المادة وهي الصورة؛ وهذه النفس فلأنها جواهر ليست موجودة في موضوع، ولأنها جزئية ليست مقولة على موضوع
Participation is that what is the concept of a noun, whether a definition or a description, is predicated of the thing of which the noun is predicated, so the thing is described by the meaning of the noun just as it is named by its utterance. And if that is not a definition for it by these things, it becomes clear that they have neglected greatly; indeed it may become clear to you that every general meaning said of more than one is a universal, and the special meaning is a particular, and that the accident which is said of substance is what has been mentioned, and that things are either predicated of a subject not existing in a subject—and these are the universals that are substances, because they are universals they are predicated of a subject, and because they are substances they do not exist in a subject; or existing in a subject not predicated of a subject—and these are the particulars of accidents, for they are accidents existing in, and because they are particular they are not predicated of; and as for predicated of a subject and existing in a subject—these are the universals of accidents, for in relation to their particulars, like universal whiteness in relation to a certain whiteness, they are predicated of a subject, and because they are accidents they exist in a subject; and as for neither predicated of a subject nor existing in a subject—these are the particulars of substances, like Zayd and Amr, and this matter and form, and this soul, because they are substances they do not exist in a subject, and because they are particular they are not predicated of a subject.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage classifies all entities into four mutually exclusive categories based on two criteria: whether they are predicated of a subject (i.e., can be said of many) and whether they exist in a subject (i.e., inhere in another). The author begins by reminding the reader that a universal (كلي) is a meaning that applies to more than one, while a particular (جزئي) applies to one alone. The first category is substances that are universals (e.g., the genus 'animal'): they are predicated of their instances (e.g., 'Zayd is an animal') but do not exist in a subject because they are substances. The second category is accidental particulars (e.g., this specific whiteness): they exist in a subject (in a body) but are not predicated of many because they are particular. The third category is accidental universals (e.g., whiteness in general): they are predicated of many (e.g., 'this whiteness is whiteness') and exist in a subject. The fourth is substantial particulars (e.g., an individual human like Zayd): they are neither predicated of many nor exist in a subject, as they are independent substances. The author uses the example of 'universal whiteness' to illustrate how a universal accident is predicated of its particulars. This fourfold division is foundational in logic for understanding how terms relate to reality and is used in theories of definition and demonstration.

Key Terms
كلي
جزئي
مقولة على موضوع
موجود في موضوع
جوهر
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The universal 'whiteness'
C) This specific whiteness in a wall
D) The genus 'animal'

Key Takeaway
Every entity falls into one of four logical boxes defined by predication and inherence, and mastering this classification is essential for clear reasoning in Islamic sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify 'forgiveness' (maghfirah) as a divine attribute: is it a universal or particular? Is it a substance or accident? Explain using the two criteria from the passage.
?
In usul al-fiqh, the term 'sunnah' as a legal category (recommended act): is it predicated of many actions? Does it exist in a subject? Which of the four categories does it belong to? Justify your answer.

Three Definitions of Accident and Their Progressive Refinement
Author presents three definitions of accident and their distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three definitions of accident and explain which non-accidents each successive definition excludes.

Core Idea
The three definitions of accident tighten from broad to precise, each excluding a different type of 'in-existence'.

In this passage, the author of the text provides three definitions of 'accident' (عرض). Each definition adds a condition that rules out a class of things that might be falsely considered accidents. By studying this progression, you will learn how to define a logical category by successive exclusion.

Guided Path
1
Locate the three definitions
Read the Arabic and identify the three definitions marked by the letters أ, ب, ت. The first is 'موجود في موضوع', the second adds 'لا كجزء منه', the third adds 'ولا يصح قوامه من دون ما هو فيه'.
The third definition is the one the author endorses as complete.
2
Identify what the first definition includes incorrectly
The author notes that 'موجود في شيء' (existing in something) could apply to the whole being in its parts, which is metaphorical, not a genuine accident. So the first definition is too broad.
Think: a whole is said to be in its parts, but it is not an accident.
3
See what the second definition excludes
Adding 'لا كجزء منه' (not as a part) removes cases where a part is in a whole, e.g., a hand in a body, and also the relation of genus in species (e.g., animal in human).
A part is not an accident; the species is not an accident of the genus.
4
Understand the third definition's added condition
The third definition adds 'لا يصح قوامه من دون ما هو فيه' (cannot subsist without that in which it is). This excludes things that are in something but can exist independently, like time and place: a thing can change time or place but still exist.
An accident must require its subject for existence.
5
Confirm the complete definition
The third definition is the one that successfully distinguishes accident from all other 'in-existence' relations: it exists in a subject, not as a part, and cannot subsist without that subject.
This is the logical definition used in subsequent discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [في العرض] أما العرض فإنه يعرف بتعريفات مختلفة: [أ] منها هو أنه الموجود في موضوع [ب] ومنها أنه الموجود في شيء لا كجزء منه، [ت] ومنها أنه الموجود في شيء لا كجزء منه ولا يصح قوامه من دون ما هو فيه. فقولنا الموجود في شيء يقع على أشياء كثيرة على بعضها بالتواطؤ وعلى بعضها بالتشكيك وعلى بعضها بالاشتباه وإنه أي الموجود في شيء يفرق بين حال العرض وبين حال ما يقال في شيء دون حال الكل في الأجزاء كما قيل من قبل، فإن ذلك باطل. وإن كان للكل صورة ما جامعة لا توجد قائمة بنفسها لكن توجد في الأجزاء جملتها؛ إذ الكل هو الأجزاء لما مرّ، وإنه وإن كان قد يكون جوهرًا فإن الكلية في الأشياء عرض فيقال إنه ليس يمتنع أن يكون الموضوع الأول للعرض مؤلفًا من أشياء كثيرة تلك الأشياء جملتها هو الموضوع لذلك العرض من حيث هي جملة، والجملة من حيث هي جملة شيء واحد. وبالحقيقة قولهم إن الكلي في الأجزاء قول مجازي» معناه أن الكلية التي بها الكل هو ما هو في الأجزاء كان الأجزاء أشياء يعرض لها هيئة ما يكون منها الكل، وتلك الهيئة هي الكلية وإنها أي الهيئة عرض جامع والكل هو مؤلف من تلك الهيئة والأجزاء. فيكون بالعرض ما يقال للكل إنه الأجزاء أي كليته في الأجزاء وقوامه فيها؛ إذ الكل لا يمكن أن يكون في شيء ولا في أشياء أيضًا بالحقيقة. وأما العرض فإنما هو عرض؛ لأنه في شيء، فإن اتفق أن يكون بوجه ما في أشياء فليس هو عرضًا من أجل ذلك؛ بل لأنه في شيء. وأما الكل فإن كونه كلا إنما هو بحسب ما يقال مجازا إنه في أشياء لا في شيء. ثم إنَّا / إذا عنينا بقولنا الموجود في شيء أي في شيء يتقوم بنفسه دون ما يوجد فيه فإنه يفرق بين حال العرض في الموضوع وحال الصورة في المادة؛ إذ المادة لا توجد بالفعل إلا بوجود الصورة فيها. وقولنا لا كجزء منه يفرق بين ذلك وبين وجود الجزء في الكل وبين وجود طبيعة الجنس في طبيعة النوع الواحد من حيث هما طبيعتان وبين وجود عمومية النوع في عمومية الجنس من حيث هما عموميتان. وبالجملة يفرق بين حال العرض في الموضوع وبين حال الجزئي في الكلي الذي من هذه الجهة يقال للجزئي إنه في الكلي، وكذلك بينه وبين وجود المادة في المركب والصورة فيه. وقولنا لا يصح قوامه من دون ما هو فيه يفرق بين العرض في الموضوع وبين كون الشيء في الزمان وبين كون الشيء في المكان كذلك. فإن كونه في هذا وذاك لا يوجب أن لا يفارق؛ بل إن
Section on accident. As for the accident, it is known by different definitions: [A] one of them is that it is that which exists in a subject; [B] another is that it exists in something not as a part of it; [C] another is that it exists in something not as a part of it and its subsistence is not sound without that in which it is. So our saying 'existing in something' applies to many things—some of them by synonymy, some by analogy, some by confusion. And that 'existing in something' distinguishes between the state of the accident and the state of what is said to be in something, without the state of the whole in the parts, as was said before, for that is false. Even if the whole has some comprehensive form that does not exist independently but exists in the totality of its parts—since the whole is the parts, as has passed—and even if it may be a substance, the wholeness in things is an accident, so it is said that it is not impossible that the primary subject of the accident be composed of many things, the totality of which is the subject for that accident insofar as they are a totality, and the totality insofar as it is a totality is one thing. And in reality, their saying 'the universal in the parts' is a metaphorical statement, meaning that the universality by which the whole is what it is, is in the parts, the parts being things that have a certain form from which the whole comes to be, and that form is the universality, and it—i.e., the form—is an accident comprising, and the whole is composed of that form and the parts. So it is accidentally that it is said of the whole that it is the parts, i.e., its universality in the parts and its subsistence in them; for the whole cannot be in a thing nor in many things in reality. As for the accident, it is an accident because it is in something; if it happens to be in some way in many things, it is not an accident for that reason, but because it is in something. As for the whole, its being a whole is only according to what is metaphorically said that it is in many things, not in one thing. Then, if we mean by our saying 'existing in something' that it is in something that subsists by itself without that in which it exists, then it distinguishes between the state of accident in the subject and the state of form in matter, since matter does not exist actually except with the existence of form in it. Our saying 'not as a part of it' distinguishes between that and the existence of a part in the whole, and between the existence of the nature of a genus in the nature of a single species insofar as they are two natures, and between the existence of the generality of the species in the generality of the genus insofar as they are two generalities. In summary, it distinguishes between the state of accident in the subject and the state of the particular in the universal, due to which the particular is said to be in the universal, and likewise between it and the existence of matter in the composite and form in it. Our saying 'its subsistence is not sound without that in which it is' distinguishes between the accident in the subject and the existence of a thing in time and the existence of a thing in place similarly. For its being in this or that does not necessitate that it is inseparable; rather...
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a detailed treatment of the definition of 'accident' (عرض). The author lists three definitions: (1) simply 'exists in a subject', (2) 'exists in something not as a part of it', (3) 'exists in something not as a part of it and cannot subsist without that thing'. The first is too broad because the whole is said to be in its parts metaphorically. The second refines by excluding part-whole relations. The third further excludes accidents like time and place that can be separated from their 'subject'—e.g., time does not require a specific thing to exist. The author then explains each phrase's distinguishing function. 'Existing in something' separates accident from substance, as substance does not inhere. 'Not as a part' distinguishes accident from a part in a whole (e.g., hand in body) and from the relation of genus to species (e.g., 'animal' in 'human' is not like an accident). 'Cannot subsist without' distinguishes accident from things that are in something but can persist without it, like location (place) or time: a thing can change place or time but still exist, whereas whiteness cannot exist without a white body. The author also critiques the claim that a whole's universality is in its parts, showing that this is metaphorical; the whole is not actually in the parts as an accident is in a subject. The passage clarifies the precise meaning of 'in' for accidents, emphasizing that the substrate must be an independent substance. The discussion uses examples from natural philosophy (matter, form, genus, species) typical of Islamic logic.

The precise definition of accident is: something that exists in a subject, not as a part of it, and cannot subsist independently of that subject.

Distinguishing Accident from Other Relations Through Definitional Phrases
Author presents three definitions of accident and their distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how each part of the third definition of accident (existing in something, not as a part, cannot subsist independently) distinguishes accident from other types of in-existence.

Core Idea
Each phrase in the definition of accident carves out a distinct class of non-accidents.

The author not only provides definitions but also explains why each phrase is necessary. This lesson breaks down the three components of the third definition and shows what each excludes.

Guided Path
1
Analyze 'existing in something'
The phrase 'موجود في شيء' distinguishes accident from substance, because substance does not exist in a subject; it subsists by itself. It also excludes the metaphorical 'in' of the whole in its parts.
The author notes that 'existing in something' can be ambiguous, so further conditions are needed.
2
Analyze 'not as a part'
The phrase 'لا كجزء منه' excludes cases where a part exists in a whole (e.g., hand in body), where the nature of a genus exists in a species (e.g., animal in human), and where matter exists in a composite or form in matter.
These are all genuine 'in' relations, but not accidents.
3
Analyze 'cannot subsist without'
The phrase 'لا يصح قوامه من دون ما هو فيه' excludes things that are in something but can be separated, like time and place. For example, an event can change time while still existing, but whiteness cannot exist without a white body.
This condition ensures that the accident is ontologically dependent on its subject.
4
Synthesize the complete distinction
Together, the three phrases distinguish accident from all other 'in-existence' relations: substance, part-whole, genus-species, matter-form, time, and place.
This is the standard definition used in Islamic logic and theology.

The Three Conditions Defining an Accident
Author distinguishes accident-in-subject from other types of inherence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions that distinguish an accident's inherence in a subject from other types of being-in-something, and apply them to classify examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Accident's definition includes three precision cuts: subject subsists by itself, accident is not a part, and accident cannot subsist without subject.

The traditional definition of an accident ('arad) as 'something that exists in a subject' is too vague. The author sharpens it with three conditions that separate accidental inherence from other relations like form-in-matter, part-in-whole, or universal-in-particular. This lesson unpacks those conditions and shows how they work in real distinctions from Kalam and Falsafa.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of accident from the passage
Find where the author states the definition: 'existing in a thing that subsists by itself, not as a part of it, and cannot subsist without that thing'. Write down these three conditions.
The Arabic key phrase is: 'موجود في شيء يتقوم بنفسه، لا كجزء منه، لا يصح قوامه من دون ما هو فيه'.
2
Apply the first condition: subject subsists by itself
This excludes form-in-matter, because matter does not subsist without form. Also excludes the whole-in-parts (the whole's subsistence depends on parts). List other types that are excluded.
The author uses this to differentiate accident from form in matter.
3
Apply the second condition: not a part
This excludes part-in-whole, genus-in-species (as nature in nature), and species-in-genus (as generality). These are 'in' something but as a part or logical component, not as a true accident.
The author elaborates: part in whole, nature of genus in nature of species, etc.
4
Apply the third condition: inseparable subsistence
This excludes being-in-time or being-in-place, because those do not necessitate inseparability. Also excludes substance in accident, matter from form, etc. Only accident has its very subsistence depending on the subject.
The author addresses objections about absolute time, place, and matter-form inseparability, clarifying that the inseparability is of the particular existent from its particular subject.
5
Test the definition against problematic cases
Consider examples from Kalam: the attribute of knowledge ('ilm) in Allah – is it an accident? According to the definition, no, because Allah's essence does not subsist by itself in the way a subject does; knowledge is not a part and is inseparable. Similarly, the black color in a body is an accident because the body subsists by itself, black is not a part, and black cannot exist without a body.
The author's own response to doubts confirms the definition's robustness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

هو الموضوع لذلك العرض من حيث هي جملة، والجملة من حيث هي جملة شيء واحد. وبالحقيقة قولهم إن الكلي في الأجزاء قول مجازي» معناه أن الكلية التي بها الكل هو ما هو في الأجزاء كان الأجزاء أشياء يعرض لها هيئة ما يكون منها الكل، وتلك الهيئة هي الكلية وإنها أي الهيئة عرض جامع والكل هو مؤلف من تلك الهيئة والأجزاء. فيكون بالعرض ما يقال للكل إنه الأجزاء أي كليته في الأجزاء وقوامه فيها؛ إذ الكل لا يمكن أن يكون في شيء ولا في أشياء أيضًا بالحقيقة. وأما العرض فإنما هو عرض؛ لأنه في شيء، فإن اتفق أن يكون بوجه ما في أشياء فليس هو عرضًا من أجل ذلك؛ بل لأنه في شيء. وأما الكل فإن كونه كلا إنما هو بحسب ما يقال مجازا إنه في أشياء لا في شيء. ثم إنَّا / إذا عنينا بقولنا الموجود في شيء أي في شيء يتقوم بنفسه دون ما يوجد فيه فإنه يفرق بين حال العرض في الموضوع وحال الصورة في المادة؛ إذ المادة لا توجد بالفعل إلا بوجود الصورة فيها. وقولنا لا كجزء منه يفرق بين ذلك وبين وجود الجزء في الكل وبين وجود طبيعة الجنس في طبيعة النوع الواحد من حيث هما طبيعتان وبين وجود عمومية النوع في عمومية الجنس من حيث هما عموميتان. وبالجملة يفرق بين حال العرض في الموضوع وبين حال الجزئي في الكلي الذي من هذه الجهة يقال للجزئي إنه في الكلي، وكذلك بينه وبين وجود المادة في المركب والصورة فيه. وقولنا لا يصح قوامه من دون ما هو فيه يفرق بين العرض في الموضوع وبين كون الشيء في الزمان وبين كون الشيء في المكان كذلك. فإن كونه في هذا وذاك لا يوجب أن لا يفارق؛ بل إن كان، فذلك بسبب آخر. وكون العرض في الموضوع يوجب ذلك من حيث إن هذا موضوع وذلك عرض . وكذلك كون الشيء في الغاية فإنه قد يفارق الغاية نحو الإنسان في السعادة والبدن في الصحة وكذلك كون الجوهر في العرض فإنه يفارق العرض، وكذلك المادة من حيث هي المادة لا يمتنع أن يفارق الصورة، وطبيعة الجنس أيضًا قد توجد مفارقة لطبيعة النوع في نوع آخر. ولقائل أن يقول في مثل هذا الكلام كيف هو والشيء في الزمان المطلق ولا يفارق الزمان المطلق، وكذلك في المكان المطلق، وكذلك الجوهر في العرض المطلق. ثم من الأجسام ما لا يوجد إلا في المكان الذي هو فيه كالقمر في فلكه مثلا. ومن المراد ما لا يفارق الصورة مثل مادة الفلك، وإنها ليست ا من الأعراض ولا شيء من الصور إلا ويمتنع أن يفارق المادة. وقد بينتُم أن كون الشيء في المادة لا يكون كون الشيء في الموضوع، لكنا نقول إن معنى قولنا لا يمكن أن يفارق لما هو فيه أن الموجود المعين أي موجود كان؛ إذ الجدة في الشيء المعين الذي هو فيه لم يكن مفارقته لذلك المعنى بل علة قوامه هي أنه فيه لا أن يكون ذلك لزمه بعد تقومه. وبهذا تزول كل واحدة من الشبه المذكورة من الزمان والمكان والصورة والمادة وغير ذلك نحو الأعراض المطلقة فإن المطلقات بحسب الوهم؛ وكلامنا بحسب الوجود. فمنهم من قال إن الفرق بين وجود الصورة في المادة وبين وجود العرض في الموضوع هو أن الصورة تكون جزءا من المركب. وأما العرض فلا يكون جزءا من الموضوع ولا من المركب كذلك لكنه لا يكون واردا على ما قلناه؛ إذ هو فرق بين وجود العرض في الموضوع وبين وجود الصورة في المركب». وليس كلامنا فيه؛ بل الكلام في الفرق بين وجود العرض في الموضوع وبين وجود الصورة في المادة التي هي غير وجود الصورة في المركب.
But [the whole] exists in the parts collectively; for the whole is the parts as previously mentioned. And although it may sometimes be a substance, yet universality in things is an accident. So it is said: it is not impossible that the first subject of an accident be composed of many things, those things collectively being the subject of that accident insofar as they are a collective, and the collective insofar as it is a collective is one thing. And in reality, their saying that the universal is in the parts is a figurative statement, meaning that the universality by which the whole is what it is is what is in the parts, as the parts are things to which a certain configuration occurs from which the whole comes to be, and that configuration is the universality. And that configuration is a comprehensive accident, and the whole is composed of that configuration and the parts. Thus, it is accidentally that it is said of the whole that it is the parts, i.e., its universality is in the parts and its subsistence is in them; for the whole cannot be in anything nor in things truly. As for the accident, it is an accident only because it is in a thing. If it happens to be in some respect in things, it is not an accident on that account, but because it is in a thing. As for the whole, its being a whole is only according to what is figuratively said: it is in things, not in a thing. Then, when we mean by our statement 'existing in a thing' that it exists in a thing that subsists by itself without that which exists in it, it distinguishes between the state of the accident in the subject and the state of the form in matter; for matter does not exist in actuality except with the existence of the form in it. And our statement 'not as a part of it' distinguishes between that and the existence of the part in the whole, and between the existence of the nature of the genus in the nature of the single species insofar as they are two natures, and between the existence of the generality of the species in the generality of the genus insofar as they are two generalities. In summary, it distinguishes between the state of the accident in the subject and the state of the particular in the universal, in which respect it is said of the particular that it is in the universal; and similarly between it and the existence of matter in the composite and the form in it. And our statement 'its subsistence cannot be correct without that which it is in' distinguishes between the accident in the subject and the thing being in time and the thing being in place similarly. For its being in this and that does not necessitate that it cannot separate; rather, if it is so, that is due to another cause. The accident's being in the subject necessitates that insofar as this is a subject and that is an accident. Similarly, a thing's being in its end may separate from its end, like a human in happiness or a body in health; similarly, a substance's being in an accident may separate from the accident; similarly, matter insofar as it is matter is not prevented from separating from form; and the nature of the genus also may exist separate from the nature of the species in another species. An objector might say in such discourse: how is it, while a thing is in absolute time and does not separate from absolute time, and similarly in absolute place, and similarly substance in absolute accident? Then, some bodies exist only in the place they are in, like the moon in its sphere for example. And some matters do not separate from form, like the matter of the sphere, and it is not an accident, nor is there any form except that it is impossible for it to separate from matter. And you have explained that a thing's being in matter is not a thing's being in the subject. But we say: the meaning of our statement 'it cannot separate from what it is in' is that the particular existent, any existent, when it is in a particular thing that is in it, it does not separate from that particular thing; rather, the cause of its subsistence is that it is in it, not that this becomes necessary for it after its subsistence. By this, every one of the mentioned doubts about time, place, form, matter, and other things are removed, like absolute accidents, for absolute things are according to imagination, and our discourse is according to existence. Some have said that the difference between the existence of form in matter and the existence of accident in subject is that form is a part of the composite, whereas accident is not a part of the subject nor of the composite similarly. But this does not apply to what we have said, for it is a difference between the existence of accident in subject and the existence of form in the composite. Our discourse is not about that; rather, the discourse is about the difference between the existence of accident in subject and the existence of form in matter, which is different from the existence of form in the composite.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the definition of an accident ('arad) and the distinctions between its inherence in a subject and other types of being-in (being in something). The author begins by addressing a potential confusion regarding the whole and its parts: the whole is said to be in its parts, but this is metaphorical. Universality (kulliyya) is an accident that resides in the parts collectively, and the whole is composed of that accident and the parts. The accident, however, is an accident because it is in a single subject, even if that subject is a collective. Then the author elaborates on the phrase 'existing in a thing' used in the definition of accident. He explains that by adding the conditions 'in a thing that subsists by itself' and 'not as a part of it', we distinguish the accident's relation to its subject from other relations: (1) form in matter, because matter does not exist without form; (2) part in whole; (3) the nature of genus in the nature of species; (4) the generality of species in the generality of genus; (5) particular in universal; (6) matter and form in the composite. The third condition 'its subsistence cannot be correct without that which it is in' distinguishes accident from being in time or place, because time and place do not necessitate inseparability. For example, a person in happiness can be separated from happiness; similarly, substance in accident, matter from form, genus from species. The author then addresses an objection about things that seem inseparable: absolute time, absolute place, substance in absolute accident, the moon in its sphere, matter of the sphere, and form in matter. He responds that the inseparability meant is of a particular existent from the particular thing it is in; the cause of its subsistence is that it is in it, not that it becomes inseparable after subsistence. Absolute considerations are mental, while the discourse is about actual existence. Finally, he rejects a different proposed distinction (that form is a part of the composite while accident is not) because that distinguishes accident in subject from form in composite, not from form in matter, which is the intended distinction.

Key Terms
العرض (al-'arad): An accident is a concept that exists in a subject (mawdu') that subsists by itself, not as a part of it, and cannot subsist without that subject. [Note: No significant shift; this is the standard definition in Aristotelian logic.]
الموضوع (al-mawdu'): The subject is that which subsists by itself and in which the accident inheres; it is the underlying entity that supports the accident. [Note: In later texts, mawdu' can also refer to the subject of a proposition, but here it is the substrate of an accident.]
الصورة (al-sura): Form is a principle in matter, and its existence in matter is different from accident in subject; matter does not exist without form. [Note: This is the hylomorphic sense, stable across falsafa texts.]
المادة (al-mada): Matter is the underlying substrate that receives form, but it does not exist in actuality without form; it is not a subject for accident in the same way. [Note: Standard in natural philosophy; used here to contrast with subject of accident.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what distinguishes an accident's being in its subject from a form's being in matter?
A) The accident is not a part of the subject, while form is a part of the composite.
B) The accident cannot exist without the subject, while matter cannot exist without form.
C) The accident is separable from the subject, while form is inseparable from matter.
D) The subject of an accident is always a substance, while matter is not a substance.

Key Takeaway
An accident is defined as something that exists in a self-subsisting subject, not as a part of it, and cannot exist without that subject; this definition systematically excludes all other types of inherence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the three conditions from this lesson, determine whether the attribute of 'speech' (kalam) in the Ash'ari view is an accident or not, given that speech is said to be an attribute that inheres in the essence of Allah.
?
Classify the following as accident-in-subject or not: the color black in a black object; the form 'humanity' in the soul; the part 'hand' in the body; the universal 'animal' in the particular 'human'.

The Ten Categories
advanced
10 min
Refining the Definition of Accident: Excluding Matter
Refining the definition of accident by distinguishing it from matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition 'not a part of anything at all' to distinguish accident from matter in a definition.

Core Idea
Accident is defined by not being a part of any composite, while matter is a part.

The author proposes a refined definition of accident: it exists in something not as a part, and crucially, it is not a part of anything at all. This excludes matter, which is a part of the hylomorphic composite. Let's work through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial definition
The definition starts as 'accident exists in something not as a part of it'. This alone is insufficient because matter also exists in form without being a part of form.
2
Spot the counterexample
Matter is in form not as a part, yet it is not an accident. So something must be added to exclude matter.
3
Apply the added condition
The author adds that accident is not a part of anything at all—neither of the subject nor of any composite. Matter, however, is a part of the composite (matter+form).
Check the Arabic: 'لا يكون العرض جزء من شيء البتة'.
4
Test with an example
Consider a wooden chair: the wood is a subject, and the shape is an accident. The shape is not a part of the wood or of the chair as a composite (the chair is not a true composite like matter-form). So the condition works.
The author acknowledges that some accidents can be parts of accidental composites, but that is a different issue.

كان، فذلك بسبب آخر. وكون العرض في الموضوع يوجب ذلك من حيث إن هذا موضوع وذلك عرض . وكذلك كون الشيء في الغاية فإنه قد يفارق الغاية نحو الإنسان في السعادة والبدن في الصحة وكذلك كون الجوهر في العرض فإنه موجود كان؛ إذ الجدة في الشيء المعين الذي هو فيه لم يكن مفارقته لذلك المعنى بل علة قوامه هي أنه فيه لا أن يكون ذلك لزمه بعد تقومه. وبهذا تزول كل واحدة من الشبه المذكورة من الزمان والمكان والصورة والمادة وغير ذلك نحو الأعراض المطلقة فإن المطلقات بحسب الوهم؛ وكلامنا بحسب الوجود. فمنهم من قال إن الفرق بين وجود الصورة في المادة وبين وجود العرض في الموضوع هو أن الصورة تكون جزءا من المركب. وأما العرض فلا يكون جزءا من الموضوع ولا من المركب كذلك لكنه لا يكون واردا على ما قلناه؛ إذ هو فرق بين وجود العرض في الموضوع وبين وجود الصورة في المركب». وليس كلامنا فيه؛ بل الكلام في الفرق بين وجود العرض في الموضوع وبين وجود الصورة في المادة التي هي غير وجود الصورة في المركب. / فلو قلت في الرسم إن العرض موجود في شيء لا كجزء منه شيء البتة لكان [ ٣٩ ] الأمر على ما قلت إذا كان مع ذلك لا يكون العرض جزء من شيء البتة؛ لا من الموضوع ولا من المركب، وكانت الصورة جزءًا من المركب. ثم العرض قد يكون جزءًا من مركب هو من الأعراض، وقد يكون جزءًا من مركب هو منها. ومن الجواهر كالكرسي من خشب ومن عارض فيه، والخشب موضوع له بالحقيقة لا مادة. وأما أمر المادة الكائنة في صورة لا يفارق المادة إلى غيرها، فذلك مما ينتقض به هذا الرسم؛ إذ يجعله عاما لهذه المادة وللعرض أيضًا. فإن المادة مما يقال إنها في الصورة بتلك الشرائط التي مرّ ذكرها. وإن قال قائل الفرق بينهما هو أن هذه المادة في طباعها أن تقبل صورة تقوم بها لكن الصورة ليست تزول عنها فيكون ذلك قسرًا عرض لها من هذه الصورة. وأما العرض فلطباعه ما هو مقوم بالموضوع وليس في طباعه أن ينتقل عنه لم يقبل منه هذا القول؛ إذ المادة التي نحن بصددها أنها لا تقوم بلا صورة وأنها ليس في طباعها أن تقبل صورة أخرى فيكون طباعها موقوفًا على هذه الصورة؛ بل الأولى أن يقال إن المادة لكونها مادة لا يلزمها أن تكون متعلقة بصورة مقارنة لها بعينها؛ بل ربما وجب ذلك لنوعية أو طبيعية كيف كانت بعد كونها مادة. وأما العرض فتعلقه بالموضوع لأعم معانيه وهو كونه عرضًا ومما ينتقض به أيضًا أمر الأعراض التي لا تفارق لكنها ليست إنما لا تفارق؛ لأن الجوهر مقوم بالكون فيها. وأما العرض فإن معنى أنه لا يفارق أنه لا يصح قوامه بنفسه مفارقا؛ بل قوامه مستفاد مما لا يفارقه. وأما التفريق الوهمي فذلك لا يعتبر. فإن الغير قد يفرقه الوهم عن الجوهر ، ومما ينقض به أيضًا هو أن من الأعراض ما يفارق الجوهر ببطلانه. وقد بينتُم أن العرض يفارق الجوهر غير أنه ظاهر فإن المعنى من هذا أنه لا يفارق منها بنفسه دون ذلك. ومما ينقض به أيضًا
It was so due to another cause. And the existence of accident in the subject necessitates that due to the fact that this is a subject and that is an accident. Likewise, the existence of a thing in an end (ghaya) — for it may separate from the end, like a human in happiness and the body in health. Likewise, the existence of substance in accident — for it separates from the accident. Likewise, matter as matter is not prevented from separating from form, and the nature of genus may also exist separate from the nature of species in another species. And one may object to such speech: How is it with a thing in absolute time and it does not separate from absolute time, likewise in absolute place, and likewise substance in absolute accident? Then among bodies, some exist only in the place they are in, like the moon in its sphere for example. And among intended things, what does not separate from form, like the matter of the sphere, and it is not from the accidents, and there is no form except that it is impossible for it to separate from matter. And you have explained that the existence of a thing in matter is not the existence of a thing in the subject. But we say: The meaning of our statement 'it cannot separate from that in which it is' is that the specific existent, any existent, since the newness in the specific thing in which it is, its separation from that meaning is not [possible], rather the cause of its subsistence is that it is in it, not that this [separation] occurred after its subsistence. And by this, every one of the mentioned doubts regarding time, place, form, matter, and other things like absolute accidents, for the absolutes are according to imagination, but our speech is according to existence. Some said that the difference between the existence of form in matter and the existence of accident in the subject is that form is a part of the composite. As for accident, it is not a part of the subject nor of the composite likewise, but that is not relevant to what we said, for it is a difference between the existence of accident in the subject and the existence of form in the composite. And our speech is not about that; rather, the speech is about the difference between the existence of accident in the subject and the existence of form in matter, which is other than the existence of form in the composite. So if you said in the definition (rasm) that accident is something existing in something not as a part of it at all, it would be as you said, provided that accident is not a part of anything at all; not of the subject nor of the composite, and that form is a part of the composite. Then accident may be a part of a composite that is from accidents, and it may be a part of a composite that is from them. And from substances, like a chair of wood and an accident in it, and wood is essentially a subject for it, not matter. As for the matter that subsists in a form not separating from it to another, that is a counterexample to this definition; for it makes it common to this matter and also to accident. Indeed, matter is said to be in form under those conditions mentioned earlier. And if someone said the difference between them is that this matter by its nature accepts a form by which it subsists, but the form does not depart from it, so that becomes a coercion that occurred to it from this form. As for accident, its nature is that it is subsistent by the subject, and it is not in its nature to transfer from it. [This] does not accept this statement; since the matter we are dealing with does not subsist without form, and it is not in its nature to accept another form, so its nature is dependent on this form; rather, it is better to say that matter, as matter, is not necessarily attached to a specific concurrent form; rather, perhaps that is necessary due to specific or natural properties after being matter. As for accident, its attachment to the subject is for its most general meaning, namely being an accident. And among the counterexamples to this [definition] is also the matter of accidents that do not separate, but they do not separate because substance is subsistent by being in them? As for accident, the meaning of 'it does not separate' is that it cannot subsist by itself separately; rather, its subsistence is derived from what does not separate from it. As for imaginary separation, that is not considered; for the imagination may separate it from substance. And among the counterexamples also is that some accidents separate from substance by their annihilation. And you have explained that accident separates from substance, except that it is apparent, for the meaning of this is that it does not separate from it by itself without that. And among the counterexamples also
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical defense of a specific definition of accident ('arad). The author argues that accident's existence 'in a subject' (mawdu') is not merely a matter of inseparability but of essential dependence. The passage opens by clarifying causality: the accident's being in the subject is necessitated because one is subject and the other is accident. It then addresses a series of objections (shubah) that challenge the notion of 'inseparability' as a criterion. For instance, one might object that things like a human in happiness or a body in health can be 'in' something and yet separate from it, or that matter is always in form but is not an accident. The author responds by distinguishing between the actual individualized existence and the abstract absolute notion; our discussion concerns actual existence, not imaginary absolutes. The author rejects a proposed alternative distinction (that form is part of a composite while accident is not) because it conflates the relationship of matter-form with that of subject-accident. He then refines the definition: accident is that which exists in something not as a part, but this also includes matter (since matter exists in form not as a part). To avoid this, he adds that accident is not a part of anything at all, neither of the subject nor of a composite, while matter is a part of a composite (the hylomorphic compound). However, this still faces counterexamples: some accidents are parts of a composite (e.g., a wooden chair has an accident of shape), but then the wood is a subject, not matter. Also, matter that is inseparable from a specific form (like the celestial matter) might still be considered an accident by this definition. The author then considers and rejects a response based on natural disposition (tab')—that matter naturally accepts a form which it never leaves, while accident naturally depends on a subject but can transfer. He argues that matter's attachment to a particular form is not essential to its materiality but may be due to specific nature. Finally, he discusses accidents that never separate (e.g., the whiteness of a raven) but insists that their inseparability is not due to essential dependence but due to other causes. The key is that accident's subsistence is derived from something it does not separate from, whereas substance (even if inseparable) does not derive its subsistence from that in which it is. Imaginary separation is irrelevant; actual separation (by annihilation) shows that accident can separate, but that does not change its essential dependence. The entire discussion guards against confusing inseparability with essential ontological dependence.

Key Terms

Refuting the Objection: Inseparability Does Not Prove Accidentality
Responding to objections about inseparability by distinguishing essence from accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute the objection that inseparability proves something is an accident, using the author's distinction between actual existence and imaginary absolutes.

Core Idea
Inseparability is not the criterion for being an accident; essential dependence is.

The author addresses an objection: if something is always in something else (like a thing in time, or matter in form), why is it not an accident? He responds by distinguishing between actual existential dependence and mere inseparability. Let's analyze his dialectical move.

Guided Path
1
Extract the objection
The objector says: 'How can you say accident is in a subject and may separate, when some things are always in something else, like a body in place, or matter in form? If inseparability is not a criterion, then what is?'
2
Identify the author's initial response
The author says that absolutes (like absolute time, absolute place) are imaginary; our discussion is about actual existence. In reality, a specific existent is not inseparable from its subject due to the subject's nature.
Arabic: 'فإن المطلقات بحسب الوهم؛ وكلامنا بحسب الوجود'.
3
Understand the key principle
The author clarifies that the meaning of 'cannot separate' is that the existent's subsistence (qiwam) is derived from that in which it is, not that it happens to be inseparable after subsistence.
This is the core: accident is essentially dependent on the subject for its existence.
4
Apply to the counterexamples
Matter does not derive its subsistence from form; rather, form gives matter actuality. So matter is not an accident. Similarly, a body in place does not derive its subsistence from that place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

يفارق العرض، وكذلك المادة من حيث هي المادة لا يمتنع أن يفارق الصورة، وطبيعة الجنس أيضًا قد توجد مفارقة لطبيعة النوع في نوع آخر. ولقائل أن يقول في مثل هذا الكلام كيف هو والشيء في الزمان المطلق ولا يفارق الزمان المطلق، وكذلك في المكان المطلق، وكذلك الجوهر في العرض المطلق. ثم من الأجسام ما لا يوجد إلا في المكان الذي هو فيه كالقمر في فلكه مثلا. ومن المراد ما لا يفارق الصورة مثل مادة الفلك، وإنها العرض فلا يكون جزءا من الموضوع ولا من المركب كذلك لكنه لا يكون واردا على ما قلناه؛ إذ هو فرق بين وجود العرض في الموضوع وبين وجود الصورة في المركب». وليس كلامنا فيه؛ بل الكلام في الفرق بين وجود العرض في الموضوع وبين وجود الصورة في المادة التي هي غير وجود الصورة في المركب. / فلو قلت في الرسم إن العرض موجود في شيء لا كجزء منه شيء البتة لكان [ ٣٩ ] الأمر على ما قلت إذا كان مع ذلك لا يكون العرض جزء من شيء البتة؛ لا من الموضوع ولا من المركب، وكانت الصورة جزءًا من المركب. ثم العرض قد يكون جزءًا من مركب هو من الأعراض، وقد يكون جزءًا من مركب هو منها. ومن الجواهر كالكرسي من خشب ومن عارض فيه، والخشب
