أيضًا أمر الأعراض التي لا تفارق لكنها ليست إنما لا تفارق؛ لأن الجوهر مقوم بالكون فيها. وأما العرض فإن معنى أنه لا يفارق أنه لا يصح قوامه بنفسه مفارقا؛ بل قوامه مستفاد مما لا يفارقه. وأما التفريق الوهمي فذلك لا يعتبر. فإن الغير قد يفرقه الوهم عن الجوهر ، ومما ينقض به أيضًا هو أن من الأعراض ما يفارق الجوهر ببطلانه. وقد بينتُم أن العرض يفارق الجوهر غير أنه ظاهر فإن المعنى من هذا أنه لا يفارق منها بنفسه دون ذلك. ومما ينقض به أيضًا
It was so due to another cause. And the existence of accident in the subject necessitates that due to the fact that this is a subject and that is an accident. Likewise, the existence of a thing in an end (ghaya) — for it may separate from the end, like a human in happiness and the body in health. Likewise, the existence of substance in accident — for it separates from the accident. Likewise, matter as matter is not prevented from separating from form, and the nature of genus may also exist separate from the nature of species in another species. And one may object to such speech: How is it with a thing in absolute time and it does not separate from absolute time, likewise in absolute place, and likewise substance in absolute accident? Then among bodies, some exist only in the place they are in, like the moon in its sphere for example. And among intended things, what does not separate from form, like the matter of the sphere, and it is not from the accidents, and there is no form except that it is impossible for it to separate from matter. And you have explained that the existence of a thing in matter is not the existence of a thing in the subject. But we say: The meaning of our statement 'it cannot separate from that in which it is' is that the specific existent, any existent, since the newness in the specific thing in which it is, its separation from that meaning is not [possible], rather the cause of its subsistence is that it is in it, not that this [separation] occurred after its subsistence. And by this, every one of the mentioned doubts regarding time, place, form, matter, and other things like absolute accidents, for the absolutes are according to imagination, but our speech is according to existence. Some said that the difference between the existence of form in matter and the existence of accident in the subject is that form is a part of the composite. As for accident, it is not a part of the subject nor of the composite likewise, but that is not relevant to what we said, for it is a difference between the existence of accident in the subject and the existence of form in the composite. And our speech is not about that; rather, the speech is about the difference between the existence of accident in the subject and the existence of form in matter, which is other than the existence of form in the composite. So if you said in the definition (rasm) that accident is something existing in something not as a part of it at all, it would be as you said, provided that accident is not a part of anything at all; not of the subject nor of the composite, and that form is a part of the composite. Then accident may be a part of a composite that is from accidents, and it may be a part of a composite that is from them. And from substances, like a chair of wood and an accident in it, and wood is essentially a subject for it, not matter. As for the matter that subsists in a form not separating from it to another, that is a counterexample to this definition; for it makes it common to this matter and also to accident. Indeed, matter is said to be in form under those conditions mentioned earlier. And if someone said the difference between them is that this matter by its nature accepts a form by which it subsists, but the form does not depart from it, so that becomes a coercion that occurred to it from this form. As for accident, its nature is that it is subsistent by the subject, and it is not in its nature to transfer from it. [This] does not accept this statement; since the matter we are dealing with does not subsist without form, and it is not in its nature to accept another form, so its nature is dependent on this form; rather, it is better to say that matter, as matter, is not necessarily attached to a specific concurrent form; rather, perhaps that is necessary due to specific or natural properties after being matter. As for accident, its attachment to the subject is for its most general meaning, namely being an accident. And among the counterexamples to this [definition] is also the matter of accidents that do not separate, but they do not separate because substance is subsistent by being in them? As for accident, the meaning of 'it does not separate' is that it cannot subsist by itself separately; rather, its subsistence is derived from what does not separate from it. As for imaginary separation, that is not considered; for the imagination may separate it from substance. And among the counterexamples also is that some accidents separate from substance by their annihilation. And you have explained that accident separates from substance, except that it is apparent, for the meaning of this is that it does not separate from it by itself without that. And among the counterexamples also
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical defense of a specific definition of accident ('arad). The author argues that accident's existence 'in a subject' (mawdu') is not merely a matter of inseparability but of essential dependence. The passage opens by clarifying causality: the accident's being in the subject is necessitated because one is subject and the other is accident. It then addresses a series of objections (shubah) that challenge the notion of 'inseparability' as a criterion. For instance, one might object that things like a human in happiness or a body in health can be 'in' something and yet separate from it, or that matter is always in form but is not an accident. The author responds by distinguishing between the actual individualized existence and the abstract absolute notion; our discussion concerns actual existence, not imaginary absolutes. The author rejects a proposed alternative distinction (that form is part of a composite while accident is not) because it conflates the relationship of matter-form with that of subject-accident. He then refines the definition: accident is that which exists in something not as a part, but this also includes matter (since matter exists in form not as a part). To avoid this, he adds that accident is not a part of anything at all, neither of the subject nor of a composite, while matter is a part of a composite (the hylomorphic compound). However, this still faces counterexamples: some accidents are parts of a composite (e.g., a wooden chair has an accident of shape), but then the wood is a subject, not matter. Also, matter that is inseparable from a specific form (like the celestial matter) might still be considered an accident by this definition. The author then considers and rejects a response based on natural disposition (tab')—that matter naturally accepts a form which it never leaves, while accident naturally depends on a subject but can transfer. He argues that matter's attachment to a particular form is not essential to its materiality but may be due to specific nature. Finally, he discusses accidents that never separate (e.g., the whiteness of a raven) but insists that their inseparability is not due to essential dependence but due to other causes. The key is that accident's subsistence is derived from something it does not separate from, whereas substance (even if inseparable) does not derive its subsistence from that in which it is. Imaginary separation is irrelevant; actual separation (by annihilation) shows that accident can separate, but that does not change its essential dependence. The entire discussion guards against confusing inseparability with essential ontological dependence.

The decisive factor in defining accident is whether the thing's subsistence depends on the subject, not whether it can actually separate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the famous kalam argument 'al-dalil al-tamani', it is argued that the world is composed of substances and accidents, and accidents are temporally originated. Suppose someone objects that celestial matter is inseparable from its form but is not an accident. How would you respond using the author's distinction?
?

Refutation of Accident Definition: The Matter Counterexample
Author refutes a definition of accident by showing it applies to matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the flaw in the definition of accident as 'existing in something not as a part' by applying the counterexample of matter.

Core Idea
Matter also subsists in a form not as a part, so the definition is too broad.

The author begins by stating a definition of accident: something that exists in a subject not as a part. He then immediately shows that this definition also applies to matter (mādda), which is not an accident. This lesson examines that specific refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition under attack
Locate the definition of accident given as 'existing in something not as a part of it at all'.
The author assumes this definition for refutation.
2
Extract the conditions
Note the definition includes: (i) exists in something, (ii) not as a part of that thing, and (iii) also not a part of any composite.
The author adds the condition that the accident is not a part of anything.
3
Find the counterexample: matter
The author claims that matter (mādda) also fits this definition: matter subsists in a form not as a part, and matter is not a part of the composite in the same way.
Matter is a substance, not an accident.
4
Assess the refutation
Does the definition also apply to matter? If yes, the definition is too broad and fails to distinguish accidents from matter.
This is a classic test of definitional boundaries: exclude non-instances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو قلت في الرسم إن العرض موجود في شيء لا كجزء منه شيء البتة لكان الأمر على ما قلت إذا كان مع ذلك لا يكون العرض جزء من شيء البتة؛ لا من الموضوع ولا من المركب، وكانت الصورة جزءًا من المركب. ثم العرض قد يكون جزءًا من مركب هو من الأعراض، وقد يكون جزءًا من مركب هو منها. ومن الجواهر كالكرسي من خشب ومن عارض فيه، والخشب موضوع له بالحقيقة لا مادة. وأما أمر المادة الكائنة في صورة لا يفارق المادة إلى غيرها، فذلك مما ينتقض به هذا الرسم؛ إذ يجعله عاما لهذه المادة وللعرض أيضًا. فإن المادة مما يقال إنها في الصورة بتلك الشرائط التي مرّ ذكرها. وإن قال قائل الفرق بينهما هو أن هذه المادة في طباعها أن تقبل صورة تقوم بها لكن الصورة ليست تزول عنها فيكون ذلك قسرًا عرض لها من هذه الصورة. وأما العرض فلطباعه ما هو مقوم بالموضوع وليس في طباعه أن ينتقل عنه لم يقبل منه هذا القول؛ إذ المادة التي نحن بصددها أنها لا تقوم بلا صورة وأنها ليس في طباعها أن تقبل صورة أخرى فيكون طباعها موقوفًا على هذه الصورة؛ بل الأولى أن يقال إن المادة لكونها مادة لا يلزمها أن تكون متعلقة بصورة مقارنة لها بعينها؛ بل ربما وجب ذلك لنوعية أو طبيعية كيف كانت بعد كونها مادة. وأما العرض فتعلقه بالموضوع لأعم معانيه وهو كونه عرضًا ومما ينتقض به أيضًا أمر الأعراض التي لا تفارق لكنها ليست إنما لا تفارق؛ لأن الجوهر مقوم بالكون فيها. وأما العرض فإن معنى أنه لا يفارق أنه لا يصح قوامه بنفسه مفارقا؛ بل قوامه مستفاد مما لا يفارقه. وأما التفريق الوهمي فذلك لا يعتبر. فإن الغير قد يفرقه الوهم عن الجوهر، ومما ينقض به أيضًا هو أن من الأعراض ما يفارق الجوهر ببطلانه. وقد بينتُم أن العرض يفارق الجوهر غير أنه ظاهر فإن المعنى من هذا أنه لا يفارق منها بنفسه دون ذلك. ومما ينقض به أيضًا هو أن من الأعراض ما يفارق منها بنفسه دون الجوهر نحو الحرارة مثلًا لكن الحرارة في الهواء لا يتكون عن النار؛ بل على سبيل حدوث الحرارة أخرى في الهواء، وإنه مما يصح في الطبيعي، فاشتغال المنطقي ببيانه خروج عن صناعته.
For if you said in the definition (rasm) that accident is existent in something not as a part of it at all, the matter would be as you said if, along with that, the accident is not a part of anything at all—neither of the subject nor of the composite—and the form is a part of the composite. Then the accident may be a part of a composite that is of accidents, and may be a part of a composite that is of them (i.e., substances). And from substances is like the chair of wood and an accident in it, and wood is truly a subject for it, not matter. As for the matter that subsists in a form not separating from matter to something else, that is something by which this definition is refuted; for it makes it general for this matter and for the accident as well. For matter is said to be in the form under those conditions previously mentioned. And if someone said the difference between them is that this matter is by its nature to accept a form by which it subsists, but the form does not depart from it, so that becomes a coercion that occurs to it from this form. As for the accident, by its nature it is something subsisting by the subject, and it is not in its nature to transfer from it—this statement is not accepted from him; for the matter we are dealing with does not subsist without a form, and it is not in its nature to accept another form, so its nature is dependent on this form. Rather, it is more proper to say that matter, by being matter, does not necessitate that it be attached to a particular form concomitant with it; rather, that may be necessitated by a specific nature or a natural property after it becomes matter. As for the accident, its attachment to the subject is for its most general meaning, which is its being an accident. And among the things by which this definition is also refuted is the matter of accidents that do not separate, but they are not non-separating because the substance is subsistent by being in them. As for the accident, the meaning of its not separating is that its subsistence is not valid by itself separately; rather, its subsistence is derived from what it does not separate from. As for imaginary separation, that is not considered. For something else may separate it by imagination from the substance. And among the things by which it is also refuted is that some accidents separate from the substance by their annihilation. And you have shown that the accident separates from the substance, but it is apparent because the meaning of this is that it does not separate from it by itself without that. And among the things by which it is also refuted is that some accidents separate from it by themselves apart from the substance, such as heat, for example, but heat in air does not arise from fire; rather, it is by way of the occurrence of another heat in the air, and this is something valid in natural science; so the logician's occupation with its explanation is a departure from his craft.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a definition (rasm) of 'accident' (عرض) given as 'existing in something not as a part of it at all'. The author points out multiple problems. First, matter (مادة) also fits this definition: matter subsists in a form not as a part, yet matter is not an accident. Second, some accidents are inseparable from the subject, and the definition fails to distinguish them from substance. Third, some accidents separate from the subject by ceasing to exist, which contradicts the idea that accident is inseparable. Fourth, some accidents like heat can separate from the substrate (e.g., air heating from fire), but the author argues this is a physical phenomenon beyond logic. The author follows each objection with clarification, ultimately showing the definition is too broad.

مادة
رسم

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the definition of accident as 'existing in something not as a part' fail?
A) It also applies to matter.
B) It also applies to substance.
C) It excludes inseparable accidents.
D) It is too narrow.

Key Takeaway
The definition of accident as 'existing in something not as a part' is too broad because it also applies to matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition 'accident is that which subsists in a subject' (as used by some mutakallimun). Does this definition also apply to matter? Write a brief critique using the same approach as the author.
?
In usul al-fiqh, the definition of 'hukm shar'i' is 'the address of the Lawgiver concerning the actions of the mukallaf by way of demand or option'. Test this definition: does it include actions of non-mukallaf? Does it exclude actions not addressed? Identify if it is too broad or too narrow.

Further Refutations of Accident Definition: Inseparable Accidents and Separation by Annihilation
Author refutes a definition of accident by showing it applies to matter and inseparable properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify two additional ways a definition of accident can fail: by including inseparable accidents and by excluding accidents that separate by annihilation.

Core Idea
The definition of accident as inseparable is challenged by accidents that do separate, and by accidents that are inseparable but in a different way from substance.

The author continues his refutation by showing that the definition also fails for inseparable accidents and for accidents that cease to exist. This lesson examines these further objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second counterexample
The author mentions 'accidents that do not separate' but are not non-separating because the substance is subsistent by being in them.
He distinguishes between inseparable accidents and the nature of substance.
2
Understand the third counterexample
Some accidents separate from the substance by their annihilation, meaning they cease to exist, which shows they are not inherently inseparable.
The author notes that 'you have shown that the accident separates from the substance' in a different sense.
3
Examine the fourth counterexample
Some accidents like heat separate from the substance by themselves (e.g., heat in air), but the author says this is a physical phenomenon and logicians should not overstep.
This shows the limits of logical definitions: they should not rely on disputable physical theories.
4
Synthesize the refutations
These counterexamples collectively show that the definition fails to uniquely identify accidents across all cases.
The author's method is to test the definition against various scenarios from metaphysics and natural science.

فلو قلت في الرسم إن العرض موجود في شيء لا كجزء منه شيء البتة لكان الأمر على ما قلت إذا كان مع ذلك لا يكون العرض جزء من شيء البتة؛ لا من الموضوع ولا من المركب، وكانت الصورة جزءًا من المركب. ثم العرض قد يكون جزءًا من مركب هو من الأعراض، وقد يكون جزءًا من مركب هو منها. ومن الجواهر كالكرسي من خشب ومن عارض فيه، والخشب موضوع له بالحقيقة لا مادة. وأما أمر المادة الكائنة في صورة لا يفارق المادة إلى غيرها، فذلك مما ينتقض به هذا الرسم؛ إذ يجعله عاما لهذه المادة وللعرض أيضًا. فإن المادة مما يقال إنها في الصورة بتلك الشرائط التي مرّ ذكرها. وإن قال قائل الفرق بينهما هو أن هذه المادة في طباعها أن تقبل صورة تقوم بها لكن الصورة ليست تزول عنها فيكون ذلك قسرًا عرض لها من هذه الصورة. وأما العرض فلطباعه ما هو مقوم بالموضوع وليس في طباعه أن ينتقل عنه لم يقبل منه هذا القول؛ إذ المادة التي نحن بصددها أنها لا تقوم بلا صورة وأنها ليس في طباعها أن تقبل صورة أخرى فيكون طباعها موقوفًا على هذه الصورة؛ بل الأولى أن يقال إن المادة لكونها مادة لا يلزمها أن تكون متعلقة بصورة مقارنة لها بعينها؛ بل ربما وجب ذلك لنوعية أو طبيعية كيف كانت بعد كونها مادة. وأما العرض فتعلقه بالموضوع لأعم معانيه وهو كونه عرضًا ومما ينتقض به أيضًا أمر الأعراض التي لا تفارق لكنها ليست إنما لا تفارق؛ لأن الجوهر مقوم بالكون فيها. وأما العرض فإن معنى أنه لا يفارق أنه لا يصح قوامه بنفسه مفارقا؛ بل قوامه مستفاد مما لا يفارقه. وأما التفريق الوهمي فذلك لا يعتبر. فإن الغير قد يفرقه الوهم عن الجوهر، ومما ينقض به أيضًا هو أن من الأعراض ما يفارق الجوهر ببطلانه. وقد بينتُم أن العرض يفارق الجوهر غير أنه ظاهر فإن المعنى من هذا أنه لا يفارق منها بنفسه دون ذلك. ومما ينقض به أيضًا هو أن من الأعراض ما يفارق منها بنفسه دون الجوهر نحو الحرارة مثلًا لكن الحرارة في الهواء لا يتكون عن النار؛ بل على سبيل حدوث الحرارة أخرى في الهواء، وإنه مما يصح في الطبيعي، فاشتغال المنطقي ببيانه خروج عن صناعته.
For if you said in the definition (rasm) that accident is existent in something not as a part of it at all, the matter would be as you said if, along with that, the accident is not a part of anything at all—neither of the subject nor of the composite—and the form is a part of the composite. Then the accident may be a part of a composite that is of accidents, and may be a part of a composite that is of them (i.e., substances). And from substances is like the chair of wood and an accident in it, and wood is truly a subject for it, not matter. As for the matter that subsists in a form not separating from matter to something else, that is something by which this definition is refuted; for it makes it general for this matter and for the accident as well. For matter is said to be in the form under those conditions previously mentioned. And if someone said the difference between them is that this matter is by its nature to accept a form by which it subsists, but the form does not depart from it, so that becomes a coercion that occurs to it from this form. As for the accident, by its nature it is something subsisting by the subject, and it is not in its nature to transfer from it—this statement is not accepted from him; for the matter we are dealing with does not subsist without a form, and it is not in its nature to accept another form, so its nature is dependent on this form. Rather, it is more proper to say that matter, by being matter, does not necessitate that it be attached to a particular form concomitant with it; rather, that may be necessitated by a specific nature or a natural property after it becomes matter. As for the accident, its attachment to the subject is for its most general meaning, which is its being an accident. And among the things by which this definition is also refuted is the matter of accidents that do not separate, but they are not non-separating because the substance is subsistent by being in them. As for the accident, the meaning of its not separating is that its subsistence is not valid by itself separately; rather, its subsistence is derived from what it does not separate from. As for imaginary separation, that is not considered. For something else may separate it by imagination from the substance. And among the things by which it is also refuted is that some accidents separate from the substance by their annihilation. And you have shown that the accident separates from the substance, but it is apparent because the meaning of this is that it does not separate from it by itself without that. And among the things by which it is also refuted is that some accidents separate from it by themselves apart from the substance, such as heat, for example, but heat in air does not arise from fire; rather, it is by way of the occurrence of another heat in the air, and this is something valid in natural science; so the logician's occupation with its explanation is a departure from his craft.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a definition (rasm) of 'accident' (عرض) given as 'existing in something not as a part of it at all'. The author points out multiple problems. First, matter (مادة) also fits this definition: matter subsists in a form not as a part, yet matter is not an accident. Second, some accidents are inseparable from the subject, and the definition fails to distinguish them from substance. Third, some accidents separate from the subject by ceasing to exist, which contradicts the idea that accident is inseparable. Fourth, some accidents like heat can separate from the substrate (e.g., air heating from fire), but the author argues this is a physical phenomenon beyond logic. The author follows each objection with clarification, ultimately showing the definition is too broad.

مادة

C) The definition does not account for the fact that some accidents are inseparable from the subject in a way different from substance.
D) Inseparable accidents are part of the substance.

Key Takeaway
The definition of accident as 'existing in something not as a part' also fails for inseparable accidents because it does not distinguish their mode of inseparability from that of substance.

Distinguishing Two Modes of Predication: Said-of vs. Existent-in
Author explains how predication chains work with genus, species, and individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the predication modes 'said of a subject' and 'existent in a subject', and trace how a universal predicated of a species applies to individuals.

Core Idea
Predication is not uniform: universals said-of subjects, accidents exist-in subjects, and the genus is an accident in the abstracted nature.

In this passage, the author clarifies two fundamental ways something can be predicated: as a universal said of a subject (e.g., 'animal' of 'human') and as an accident existing in a subject (e.g., whiteness in a body). Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing definitions and categories in logic. The author also shows how a predication chain works: if animal is said of human, and human is said of Zayd, then animal is said of Zayd. However, the genus itself—when considered as a universal—is an accident in the abstract nature, not a direct predication of the species in the same way.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two predication types
From the text, define 'said of a subject' (القول على الموضوع) and 'existent in a subject' (الوجود في الموضوع). Note the example: animal said of human, and white existing in a subject.
The first is for universals (species, genera), the second for accidents.
2
Trace the predication chain
Follow the example: animal is predicated of human as said-of, and human is predicated of Zayd as said-of. Conclude that animal is therefore predicated of Zayd as said-of. This shows transitivity in said-of predication.
The chain applies only for said-of, not for existent-in.
3
Distinguish genus from nature
The author argues that the genus (e.g., animal as a universal) is not directly predicated of the nature of animal; rather, the nature becomes a genus only when considered abstractly in the mind. The genus is an accident inhering in that nature, like whiteness in a body.
This prevents confusing the nature itself with the universal predicate.
4
Apply the distinction to logical analysis
Recognize that when we say 'animal is a genus', we are not saying that the nature of animal is a genus per se, but that the mental abstraction of animal with the condition of commonality is a genus. This is key in definitions and categorizations.
This distinction is used in Kalam when discussing attributes of God.

Section: On the compositions occurring between 'said of a subject' and 'existent in a subject'. As for the compositions occurring between 'said of a subject' and 'existent in a subject', we say: when something is predicated of something else as a predication of 'said of a subject', then something else is predicated of that thing likewise. Then this is predicated of that which the first was predicated of, just as when 'animal' is predicated of 'human' as a predication of 'said of a subject', and 'human' is predicated of Zayd and Amr, and likewise. So 'animal' is predicated of Zayd and Amr as a predication of 'said of a subject' as well, since Zayd is an animal and shares with it in its definition, i.e., its definition is predicated of him. And it is not said how is that, while the genus is predicated of animal and animal is predicated of human; but the genus is not predicated of human, for the genus is not predicated of the nature of animal—rather, its nature is not a genus at all, otherwise every animal would be a genus. For the animal that has genus predicated of it is the nature of animal considered in the mind as abstracted such that it is suitable for the occurrence of commonality in it. And this consideration is more specific than the consideration of animal merely as animal, because it is with the condition of abstraction without that. But it is called 'genus' only if considered in the mind with the condition of mixing with the expression and reaching a mixing in potentiality, and animal is not with this condition; and with the condition of abstraction, it is predicated of human; rather, without the condition of mixing. And 'genus' is an accident in this nature, existing in it as a thing exists in a subject. As for the genus itself, its predication on what it is predicated of from this nature is a predication composed of accident, and the subject of predication is like the predication of 'white' on Zayd. And in reality...
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the relationship between two modes of predication: 'said of a subject' (القول على الموضوع) and 'existent in a subject' (الوجود في الموضوع), which correspond to the predication of universals and accidents. The author explains that when a universal is predicated of a species as 'said of a subject', and that species is predicated of individuals, then the universal also applies to those individuals by the same mode. For example, 'animal' is said of 'human', and 'human' is said of Zayd and Amr; thus 'animal' is said of Zayd and Amr as well. However, the genus (e.g., 'animal') is not predicated of the nature of animal when considered as a genus; only the abstracted nature suitable for commonality is called genus. The author distinguishes between the nature considered with conditions of abstraction (allowing it to be a genus) and without. The genus is an accident inhering in that nature. Finally, the predication of a genus is like the predication of a white thing on Zayd—a composite predication involving an accident. The text breaks off, likely continuing to discuss further compositions.

Refuting the claim that one thing can be both substance and accident
Author refutes the notion that a single thing can be both substance and accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical error in claiming that a single entity is both substance and accident, and explain why the definitions of substance and accident preclude this.

Core Idea
Substance and accident are defined by mutual exclusivity: one exists independently, the other dependently.

This lesson examines a common confusion in the logical tradition: the idea that the same thing could be both a substance (جوهر) and an accident (عرض). The author dismantles this by clarifying the definitions and exposing the misuse of homonymy (اشتراك). Understanding this refutation sharpens your ability to evaluate category distinctions, a skill essential for sound reasoning in Islamic theology and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definitions of substance and accident
Read the passage where the author defines substance as 'that whose essence exists without existing in anything' and accident as 'that whose essence does not exist without existing in a thing.' These definitions are exclusive; something cannot both require a subject and not require a subject.
These definitions are foundational for the entire argument.
2
Identify the proponents' arguments
The proponents used three examples: (1) differentiae of substances are called both substances and qualities; (2) form in a substrate is not a part, so it is an accident, yet it is a part of the composite substance; (3) whiteness is part of a white substance but an accident in its subject.
Each argument aims to show the same thing playing contradictory roles.
3
See how the author exposes homonymy
The author points out that the term 'differentia' is used homonymously: differentiae of substances are not qualities in the categorical sense. The proponents conflated the two meanings, leading to the false conclusion that the same thing is both substance and accident.
This is a classic error of equivocation.
4
Apply the definitions to refute the claim
The author concludes by stating that the definitions are contradictory: a single thing cannot be both dependent and independent in existence. Therefore, all the proponents' examples are dismissed as 'corrupt conjectures and false analogies.'
This step reinforces the logical principle that definitions determine possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في كون الشيء الواحد جوهرًا وعرضًا ] وأما قول من قال إن الشيء الواحد يكون جوهرًا وعرضًا لما أن الصورة في موضوع، والموضوع يقال بالاشتراك على ما مرَّ ذكره، وعلى المادة؛ بل على ما يعم الموضع والمادة، فذلك باطل؛ لأنهم لما سمعوا أن فصول الجواهر جواهر وسمعوا أن فصول الجواهر كيفيات، ولم يعلموا أن فصول الجواهر إنما سميت بهذا بالاشتراك فظنوا أن الكيفية التي هي المقولة مشتملة على فصول الجواهر، وكانت هذه الكيفية عرضًا فصارت فصول الجواهر أعراضًا. وكانت فصول الجواهر عندهم أيضًا جواهر فكان الشيء الواحد يكون عرضًا وجوهرًا. وأيضًا أن الصورة في حامل الصورة لا كجزء منه فكانت عرضًا، وكانت في الجوهر المركب جزءًا منه فكانت جوهرًا؛ إذ جزء الجوهر جوهر، فكان الشيء الواحد جوهرًا وعرضًا. والبياض أيضًا جزء من الأبيض الذي هو جوهر ولم يكن فيه على نحو وجود العرض في الشيء فهو فيه إذن جوهر، وهو بعينه في موضوعه عرض؛ إذ هو فيه لا كجزء منه فيكون جوهرًا وعرضًا؛ فهذه كلها من الظنون الفاسدة والمقايس الباطلة. وكيف لا ؟ والمعنى بالجوهر هو الذي توجد ذاته من غير أن يوجد في شيء البتة، وبالعرض هو الذي لا توجد ذاته من غير أن يوجد في الشيء البتة. ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن أن يكون الشيء الواحد جوهرًا وعرضًا، فإن من المحال أن يكون الشيء مفتقرا في وجوده إلى ما يوجد فيه ولا يكون مفتقرا. وأما قولهم إن الصورة في حامل الصورة / لا كجزء منه فكانت عرضا»، فنقول إنها لا تكون عرضًا البتة وإلا لكانت في موضوع، وذلك ممتنع ؛ لأنها إما في المادة وقد بينا أنها ليست فيها كالشيء في الموضوع، وإما في المركب من
Section: [On a single thing being both substance and accident]. As for the statement of those who say that a single thing is both substance and accident because form is in a subject, and 'subject' is said by homonymy as previously mentioned, both for matter and for what encompasses place and matter, that is false. Because when they heard that the differentiae of substances are substances and that the differentiae of substances are qualities, and they did not know that the differentiae of substances are only so called by homonymy, they thought that quality (which is a category) includes the differentiae of substances. This quality is an accident, so the differentiae of substances became accidents. And the differentiae of substances in their view were also substances, so the same thing became both accident and substance. Also, form in its substrate is not as a part of it, so it is an accident; but in the composite substance it is a part of it, so it is a substance—since a part of a substance is a substance—so the same thing is both substance and accident. Also, whiteness is a part of the white object which is a substance, and it is not in it in the manner of an accident's existence in a thing; therefore it is in it as a substance, and it itself in its subject is an accident, since it is in it not as a part, so it is both substance and accident. All these are corrupt conjectures and false analogies. How could it be? The meaning of substance is that whose essence exists without existing in anything at all, and accident is that whose essence does not exist without existing in a thing at all. If that is the case, then it is impossible for one thing to be both substance and accident, for it is impossible for a thing to be dependent in its existence on that in which it exists and also not be dependent. As for their statement that form in its substrate is not as a part of it, so it is an accident, we say: it is not an accident at all, for then it would be in a subject—which is impossible. Because it is either in matter, and we have shown that it is not in it as a thing in a subject, or it is in the composite...
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the claim that a single thing can be both a substance (جوهر) and an accident (عرض). The author identifies and dismantles two main arguments put forward by proponents of this view. First, they argue that the differentiae of substances (فصول الجواهر) are called both 'substances' and 'qualities' (كيفيات), leading to the conclusion that they are both substances and accidents. The author counters that this relies on homonymy (اشتراك) in the term 'differentia'; in reality, the differentiae of substances are not qualities in the categorical sense. Second, the proponents argue that form (صورة) exists in its substrate not as a part, so it is an accident, yet in the composite substance it is a part, so it is a substance. Similarly, whiteness (بياض) is part of a white substance but is an accident in its subject. The author declares these all to be 'corrupt conjectures and false analogies.' He then provides the decisive refutation by citing the definitions: substance is that whose essence exists without existing in anything, while accident is that whose essence does not exist without existing in a thing. These definitions are mutually exclusive; a single thing cannot both depend on something for existence and not depend on it. Finally, he addresses the claim about form in its substrate: form is not an accident at all, because if it were, it would have to be in a subject, which is impossible. Form in matter and form in the composite are not accidents in the strict sense.

Key Terms
جوهر
عرض
صورة
موضوع
اشتراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because substances are eternal while accidents are temporal.
B) Because the definitions of substance and accident are mutually exclusive: one exists independently, the other dependently.
C) Because accidents cannot exist without substances, but substances can exist without accidents.
D) Because the proponents of this view relied on corrupt analogies.

Key Takeaway
A single thing cannot be both substance and accident because their definitions are contradictory: one exists independently, the other dependently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the claim that 'knowledge' is both a substance (because it exists in the mind independently) and an accident (because it inheres in the knower). Using the definitions from this lesson, explain why this claim is false.
?

15 min
Refuting the Claim that One Thing is Both Substance and Accident
Author refutes the claim that a single thing can be both substance and accident by analyzing equivocal terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify equivocal terms in arguments about substance and accident and refute the claim that one thing can be both.

Core Idea
Substance and accident are mutually exclusive by definition.

A common error in theology and philosophy is to think that a single thing could be both a substance and an accident. This mistake often arises from equivocal language, where a term like 'quality' is used in different senses. The author of this passage systematically dismantles such arguments by clarifying definitions and exposing the equivocation. By learning to spot these fallacies, you will sharpen your ability to defend clear metaphysical distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definitions of substance and accident
Note the author's definitions: substance exists without being in a subject; accident exists only in a subject. Write them down. These are the foundation of the refutation.
These definitions are stable and widely accepted in Islamic philosophy.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponent claims that a single thing (e.g., the differentia of a substance) can be both substance and accident because it is called a 'quality' and quality is an accident, while also being a differentia of substance.
The opponent relies on the term 'quality' being applied to both the category of quality and to simple differentiae.
3
Locate the equivocation
The author points out that 'quality' is said equivocally (bi-al-ishtirak) when applied to differentiae versus the category of quality. The opponent did not realize this and thus conflated two distinct meanings.
Equivocation invalidates the argument because the middle term is not univocal.
4
Apply the definitions to test the opponent's examples
Check the opponent's examples (form in matter, whiteness in a white substance) against the definitions. The author shows that none of these are accidents: form in matter is not in a subject, and whiteness as a part of a substance is not an accident. Therefore, the claim fails.
Use the definition to determine whether something is in a subject as an accident or as a part.
5
Understand the critique's conclusion
The author concludes that the differentiae of substances (like rationality) are not accidents at all, and calling them 'quality' is merely equivocal. Thus, no single thing is both substance and accident.
The refutation is complete: the opponent's argument rests on a false assumption due to equivocation.

فصل : [ في كون الشيء الواحد جوهرًا وعرضًا ] وأما قول من قال إن الشيء الواحد يكون جوهرًا وعرضًا لما أن الصورة في موضوع، والموضوع يقال بالاشتراك على ما مرَّ ذكره، وعلى المادة؛ بل على ما يعم الموضع والمادة، فذلك باطل؛ لأنهم لما سمعوا أن فصول الجواهر جواهر وسمعوا أن فصول الجواهر كيفيات، ولم يعلموا أن فصول الجواهر إنما سميت بهذا بالاشتراك فظنوا أن الكيفية التي هي المقولة مشتملة على فصول الجواهر، وكانت هذه الكيفية عرضًا فصارت فصول الجواهر أعراضًا. وكانت فصول الجواهر عندهم أيضًا جواهر فكان الشيء الواحد يكون عرضًا وجوهرًا. وأيضًا أن الصورة في حامل الصورة لا كجزء منه فكانت عرضًا، وكانت في الجوهر المركب جزءًا منه فكانت جوهرًا؛ إذ جزء الجوهر جوهر، فكان الشيء الواحد جوهرًا وعرضًا. والبياض أيضًا جزء من الأبيض الذي هو جوهر ولم يكن فيه على نحو وجود العرض في الشيء فهو فيه إذن جوهر، وهو بعينه في موضوعه عرض؛ إذ هو فيه لا كجزء منه فيكون جوهرًا وعرضًا؛ فهذه كلها من الظنون الفاسدة والمقاييس الباطلة. وكيف لا ؟ والمعنى بالجوهر هو الذي توجد ذاته من غير أن يوجد في شيء البتة، وبالعرض هو الذي لا توجد ذاته إلا في شيء البتة. ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن أن يكون الشيء الواحد جوهرًا وعرضًا، فإن من المحال أن يكون الشيء مفتقرا في وجوده إلى ما يوجد فيه ولا يكون مفتقرا. وأما قولهم إن الصورة في حامل الصورة لا كجزء منه فكانت عرضا، فنقول إنها لا تكون عرضًا البتة وإلا لكانت في موضوع، وذلك ممتنع؛ لأنها إما في المادة وقد بينا أنها ليست فيها كالشيء في الموضوع، وإما في المركب من المادة وغيرها. وإنها في المركب كجزء منه فلا يكون فيه كالشيء في الموضوع أيضًا، وحينئذ يلزم أن لا يكون لها وجود في شيء يتوهم أنها فيه كالشيء في الموضوع، فلا يكون عرضًا إذن؛ بل هي جوهر على الإطلاق. وأما فصول الجواهر أعني الفصول البسيطة التي لا تحمل على الجواهر كالنطق وغير ذلك، فإنها أيضًا ليست في شيء من الأشياء نحو العرض في موضوع لا في النوع فإنه جزءه، ولا في الجنس، فإن طبيعة الجنس ليست موضوعة ولا مادة بالحقيقة كما تعلمه في مواضعه. ثم الجنس إن كان له طبيعة معينة يتصور بالفصل من خارج كان هو كالمادة التي إنما تتقوم بالصورة بالفعل، وكان الفصل كالصورة فلم يكن عرضًا البتة، ولا من المقولة التي هي الكيفية؛ بل إذا قيل له كيفية فذلك بالاشتراك؛ إذ الكيفية بما يقال بالاشتراك على أشياء فتقع في مقولات مختلفة فيسمى كل قوة وكل مبتدأ فعل وكل شيء يخصص شيئًا كيفية، وليست المقولة كمية أو كيفية أو غير ذلك إلا واحدًا من معاني الاسم المشترك، وذلك لا يكون إلا وأن يكون متقوما بموضوعه. فإن الاسم المشترك لا يكون جنسًا البتة.
Section on whether a single thing is substance and accident. As for the statement of one who said that a single thing can be substance and accident because the form is in a subject, and 'subject' is said equivocally as previously mentioned and to matter, rather to what encompasses place and matter, this is false. Because when they heard that the differentiae of substances are substances and heard that the differentiae of substances are qualities, and they did not know that the differentiae of substances are only called so equivocally, they thought that the category quality includes the differentiae of substances, and this quality is an accident, so the differentiae of substances became accidents. And the differentiae of substances according to them were also substances, so a single thing became accident and substance. Also, the form in the bearer of the form is not as a part of it, so it was an accident, and in the composite substance it is a part of it, so it was a substance, since a part of a substance is a substance, so the single thing was substance and accident. Also, whiteness is a part of the white thing which is a substance, and it is not in it in the mode of existence of an accident in a thing, so it is in it thereby a substance, and it itself in its subject is an accident, since it is in it not as a part of it, so it is substance and accident. All these are from false opinions and invalid syllogisms. And why not? The meaning of substance is that whose essence exists without being in any thing at all, and the meaning of accident is that whose essence does not exist except in a thing at all. And if it were so, then it is impossible that a single thing be substance and accident, for it is impossible that a thing be dependent in its existence on that in which it exists and not be dependent. As for their statement that the form in the bearer of the form is not as a part of it so it was an accident, we say: it is not an accident at all, otherwise it would be in a subject, and that is impossible, because it is either in the matter, and we have shown that it is not in it as a thing in a subject, or in the composite of matter and other than it. And it is in the composite as a part of it, so it is not in it as a thing in a subject either, and then it follows that it has no existence in a thing in which it is imagined to be as a thing in a subject, so it is not an accident then; rather it is substance absolutely. As for the differentiae of substances, I mean the simple differentiae which are not predicated of substances, like rationality and other than that, they also are not in any of the things in the mode of an accident in a subject, neither in the species for it is its part, nor in the genus, for the nature of the genus is not a subject nor matter in reality as you will learn in its places. Then the genus, if it has a determinate nature that is conceptualized by the differentia from outside, it is like the matter which is constituted by the form in actuality, and the differentia is like the form, so it is not an accident at all, nor from the category which is quality; rather if it is called quality, that is equivocally, for quality is said equivocally of things that fall under different categories, so every power and every initiator of an act and everything that specifies something is called quality, and the category quantity or quality or other than that is only one of the meanings of the equivocal name, and that only happens when it is constituted by its subject. For the equivocal name is not a genus at all.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the false doctrine that a single thing can be both substance and accident. The author identifies the source of the error: the opponents heard that the differentiae of substances are substances and also that they are qualities, and they did not realize that the term 'quality' is used equivocally when applied to differentiae. Consequently, they mistakenly included the differentiae under the accident category 'quality', leading to the contradiction. Then the author addresses another argument: the form in a bearer is not a part of it, so it is an accident, but in a composite substance it is a part, so it is substance. The author refutes this by explaining that the form is never in a subject as an accident; in matter it is not like an accident and in the composite it is a part, so it is not an accident. Next, the author discusses the simple differentiae like rationality, which are not accidents because they are not in anything as an accident; they are parts of the species or related to the genus as form to matter. Calling them 'quality' is equivocal. The passage concludes by emphasizing the impossibility of one thing being both substance and accident based on their definitions.

بالاشتراك

Key Takeaway
Substance and accident are mutually exclusive categories; any argument that conflates them using equivocal language is invalid.

Why Differentiae of Substances Are Not Accidents
Definitional argument about differentiate of substances

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the simple differentiate of substances are not classified as accidents based on their part-whole relationship.

Core Idea
Differentiae of substances are parts, not accidents.

In this passage, the author tackles a key ontological question: are the differentiate of substances (like rationality for humans) accidents? Many might assume they are because they inhere in the species. But the author argues they are not accidents—they are integral parts of the species, not dependent on a subject. To understand this, we must examine how differentiae relate to genera and species.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term 'fasl' (differentia)
The author distinguishes between simple differentiae of substances (e.g., rationality) and other differentiate. Confirm that these differentiate are not predicated of substances (i.e., 'rational' does not stand alone as a substance).
2
Analyze the relationship: differentia in species vs. accident in subject
The author says differentia is a part of the species, not something that exists in a subject as an accident does. An accident exists in a subject and is dependent on it; a part constitutes the whole.
Accident = 'arad; differentia = fasl.
3
Apply the same to genus: differentiate are not in the genus as accidents
Even if the genus is considered like matter and the differentia like form, the differentia is not an accident in the genus because the genus's nature is not a subject or matter in a way that would make the differentia accidental.
The author mentions this will be clarified elsewhere.
4
Refute the idea that differentiae are qualities by homonymy
Sometimes differentiae are called 'quality', but only by homonymy (اشتراك). The true category of quality is only applicable to items that are constituted by a subject. Differentiae are not constituted by a subject.
Homonymous names are not true genera.
5
Conclude: differentiae are substances absolutely
Since they are not in a subject as an accident, they are substances. This has implications for defining species and understanding metaphysical categories.
The conclusion aligns with the Aristotelian tradition.

The matter and others. And it is in the composite as a part of it, so it is not in it as a thing in a subject either. Then it follows that it has no existence in something in which it is imagined to be as a thing in a subject, so it is not an accident then; rather it is a substance absolutely. As for the differentiate of substances, I mean the simple differentiate that are not predicated of substances, like rationality and others, they also are not in any thing in the manner of an accident in a subject: not in the species, for it is its part, and not in the genus, for the nature of the genus is not a subject nor a matter in reality as you will learn in its places. Then if the genus has a determinate nature conceived by the differentia from outside, it is like matter that is actually constituted by form, and the differentia is like form, so it is not an accident at all, nor from the category that is quality. Rather, if it is called quality, it is by homonymy; for quality is predicated homonymously of things that fall under different categories, so every power and every principle of action is called quality, and everything that specifies something is called quality, but the category is not quantity or quality or anything else except one of the meanings of the homonymous name, and that is only when it is constituted by a subject. For the homonymous name is not a genus at all. And their saying: 'The accident in a composite is in it not as a part, and everything that is in something not as a part is not an accident in it, and everything that is not an accident in something is a substance in it' contains a compound syllogism comprising three premises. As for the first, which is his saying: 'The accident in a composite is not in it not as a part,' it is among that about which there is no dispute. As for the second, which is his saying: 'And everything that is in something not as a part is not an accident in it,' it is open to objection if what is meant is that everything in a composite with this description is not in itself an accident in it, because it is an accident as previously. But if what is meant is that it is not in it with that being its subject while it is in it as in a subject, then that is granted but it only follows that the accident is not in the composite. As for the third, which is: 'And everything that is in something and is not an accident in it is a substance in it,' it is among that about which there is no dispute if what is meant is that everything that is in something, and is not in itself an accident and is also in it, is a substance. But if what is meant is that everything that is in something not as an accident in the manner of an accident in a subject, insofar as it is in terms of being a substance in it, then that is open to objection. For it does not follow from the fact that a thing is not an accident in the thing in which it is that it is a substance in it; rather, what is not an accident in itself is a substance in itself, if substance is that which is not in a subject, whether that composite or other. Then when something is added to something in which it is as a thing in a subject, it is an accident and also accidental: it is an accident because it is inherently dependent on a subject, and it is accidental because it is something relative to this subject and not constitutive relative to it. In summary, if a thing is in itself not dependent on any subject at all, then that in which it is: if it is in itself dependent on a subject in which it is, whatever that subject may be, then it is an accident; otherwise it is a substance that exists in another as known.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about the ontological status of the differentiate (فصول) of substances, such as 'rational' for human beings. The author begins by asserting that these differentiate are not accidents because they exist in the composite as parts, not as accidents in a subject. He then addresses a potential counterargument presented as a compound syllogism with three premises. The author accepts the first premise as uncontroversial, but refutes the second premise if it intends to claim that such items are not accidents in themselves. He shows that the syllogism fails to prove that differentiate are substances in the subject; rather, they are substances in themselves but not necessarily in the subject. The passage concludes with a precise definition: if something is not inherently dependent on a subject, it is a substance; if it is in something that does depend on a subject, it is an accident. Throughout, the analysis uses technical logical terminology and is grounded in discussions of genera and species.

Analyzing a Compound Syllogism on Accident and Substance
Critique of a compound syllogism about accident and substance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three premises of the compound syllogism and evaluate the author's objection to its second premise.

Core Idea
Not every item not in a composite as a part is necessarily not an accident.

The author now examines a compound syllogism presented by opponents who argue that differentiate are substances in the subject. The syllogism has three premises, and the author systematically critiques the second premise, showing it is ambiguous and leads to false conclusions if taken unqualified. This lesson will help you understand how to identify and assess the validity of such compound arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the compound syllogism in the source
The syllogism: (1) The accident in a composite is in it not as a part. (2) Everything that is in something not as a part is not an accident in it. (3) Everything that is not an accident in something is a substance in it. Conclusion: The accident in a composite is a substance in it.
The author calls this a 'compound syllogism' because it has three premises.
2
Identify the first premise and its acceptance
The first premise says the accident in a composite is not present as a part. The author accepts this as uncontroversial.
This fits the definition of accident: it exists in a subject without being part of its essence.
3
Examine the second premise and the author's objection
The second premise: 'Everything that is in something not as a part is not an accident in it.' The author objects: if this means such items are not accidents in themselves, then it is false because they are accidents. But if it means they are not in the composite as in a subject (i.e., the composite is not their subject), then it is conceded, but only implies the accident is not in the composite.
The ambiguity lies in the phrase 'is not an accident in it' – does 'it' refer to the composite or to the item itself?
4
Assess the third premise and its dual interpretation
The third premise: 'Everything that is in something and is not an accident in it is a substance in it.' If it means 'not an accident in itself and is in the composite', then it is accepted. If it means 'not an accident in the composite as in a subject', then it is objected because not being an accident in something does not automatically make it a substance in that something.
The author distinguishes between being a substance in oneself versus being a substance in another.
5
Synthesize the correct view on differentiate
The author concludes: differentiate are substances in themselves (not dependent on a subject), but they are not substances in the composite as if the composite were their subject. They are parts of the composite, not accidents.
The syllogism fails to prove that differentiate are substances in the composite.

مادة بالحقيقة كما تعلمه في مواضعه . ثم الجنس إن كان له طبيعة معينة يتصور بالفصل من خارج كان هو كالمادة التي إنما تقوم بالصورة بالفعل، وكان الفصل كالصورة فلم يكن عرضًا ،البتة، ولا من المقولة التي هي الكيفية ؛ بل إذا قيل له كيفية فذلك بالاشتراك؛ إذ الكيفية بما يقال بالاشتراك على أشياء فتقع في مقولات مختلفة فيسمى كل قوة وكل مبتدأ فعل. وكل شيء يخصص شيئًا ،كيفية، وليست المقولة كمية أو كيفية أو غير ذلك إلا واحدًا من معاني الاسم المشترك، وذلك لا يكون إلا وأن يكون متقوما بموضوعه. فإن الاسم المشترك لا يكون جنسًا البتة. وقولهم إن العرض في المركب هو فيه ليس لا كجزء منه، وكل ما هو في شيء ليس لا كجزء منه فليس عرضًا فيه، وكل ما ليس عرضًا في شيء فهو جوهر»، فيه قياس مركب يشتمل على ثلاث مقدمات: أما الأولى وهي قوله إن العرض في المركب ليس لا كجزء منه»، فإنها من جملة ما لا نزاع فيه. وأما الثانية وهي قوله وكل ما هو في شيء ليس لا كجزء منه فليس عرضًا فيه»، فإنها في حيز المنع إذا كان المراد منه أن كل ما هو في المركب بهذه الصفة فذلك ليس في نفسه عرضًا وهو فيه؛ إذ هو عرض لما مر . وإن كان المراد منه هو أنه لا يكون فيه على أن ذلك موضوعه وهو فيه في موضوع؛ فذلك مسلم لكنه لا يلزم منه إلا وأن العرض لا يكون في المركب وأما الثالثة وهى أن كل ما كان في شيء، وليس عرضًا فيه فهو جوهر فيه، فإنها من جملة ما لا نزاع فيه إذا كان المراد منه أن كل شيء هو في شيء، وليس في نفسه عرضًا وهو أيضًا فيه، فهو جوهر. فأما إذا كان المراد منه أن كل شيء هو في شيء لا على أنه فيه نحو العرض في الموضوع بحسب أن يكون بالقياس إلى كونه فيه جوهرًا، فذلك في حيز المنع . فإنه لا يلزم من عدم كون الشيء عرضًا في الشيء الذي هو فيه فهو جوهر فيه؛ بل ما لم يكن عرضًا في نفسه فهو جوهر في نفسه إذا لم يكن الجوهر ما ليس في موضوع هو ذلك المركب أو غيره. ثم الشيء إذا أضيف إلى شيء هو فيه كالشيء / في الموضوع فهو عرض وعرضي أيضًا؛ إما أنه عرض فلكونه مفتقرًا في نفسه إلى موضوع، وإما أنه عرضي)) فلكونه أمرًا له بالقياس إلى هذا الموضوع وهو غير مقوم له بالقياس إليه. وبالجملة فالشيء إذا كان في نفسه غير مفتقر إلى موضوع ما البتة، فالذي هو فيه إن كان في نفسه مفتقرًا إلى موضوع يكون فيه أي شيء كان ذلك الموضوع فهو عرض، وإلا فهو جوهر يكون في الغير على ما عرف.
The matter and others. And it is in the composite as a part of it, so it is not in it as a thing in a subject either. Then it follows that it has no existence in something in which it is imagined to be as a thing in a subject, so it is not an accident then; rather it is a substance absolutely. As for the differentiate of substances, I mean the simple differentiate that are not predicated of substances, like rationality and others, they also are not in any thing in the manner of an accident in a subject: not in the species, for it is its part, and not in the genus, for the nature of the genus is not a subject nor a matter in reality as you will learn in its places. Then if the genus has a determinate nature conceived by the differentia from outside, it is like matter that is actually constituted by form, and the differentia is like form, so it is not an accident at all, nor from the category that is quality. Rather, if it is called quality, it is by homonymy; for quality is predicated homonymously of things that fall under different categories, so every power and every principle of action is called quality, and everything that specifies something is called quality, but the category is not quantity or quality or anything else except one of the meanings of the homonymous name, and that is only when it is constituted by a subject. For the homonymous name is not a genus at all. And their saying: 'The accident in a composite is in it not as a part, and everything that is in something not as a part is not an accident in it, and everything that is not an accident in something is a substance in it' contains a compound syllogism comprising three premises. As for the first, which is his saying: 'The accident in a composite is not in it not as a part,' it is among that about which there is no dispute. As for the second, which is his saying: 'And everything that is in something not as a part is not an accident in it,' it is open to objection if what is meant is that everything in a composite with this description is not in itself an accident in it, because it is an accident as previously. But if what is meant is that it is not in it with that being its subject while it is in it as in a subject, then that is granted but it only follows that the accident is not in the composite. As for the third, which is: 'And everything that is in something and is not an accident in it is a substance in it,' it is among that about which there is no dispute if what is meant is that everything that is in something, and is not in itself an accident and is also in it, is a substance. But if what is meant is that everything that is in something not as an accident in the manner of an accident in a subject, insofar as it is in terms of being a substance in it, then that is open to objection. For it does not follow from the fact that a thing is not an accident in the thing in which it is that it is a substance in it; rather, what is not an accident in itself is a substance in itself, if substance is that which is not in a subject, whether that composite or other. Then when something is added to something in which it is as a thing in a subject, it is an accident and also accidental: it is an accident because it is inherently dependent on a subject, and it is accidental because it is something relative to this subject and not constitutive relative to it. In summary, if a thing is in itself not dependent on any subject at all, then that in which it is: if it is in itself dependent on a subject in which it is, whatever that subject may be, then it is an accident; otherwise it is a substance that exists in another as known.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about the ontological status of the differentiate (فصول) of substances, such as 'rational' for human beings. The author begins by asserting that these differentiate are not accidents because they exist in the composite as parts, not as accidents in a subject. He then addresses a potential counterargument presented as a compound syllogism with three premises. The author accepts the first premise as uncontroversial, but refutes the second premise if it intends to claim that such items are not accidents in themselves. He shows that the syllogism fails to prove that differentiate are substances in the subject; rather, they are substances in themselves but not necessarily in the subject. The passage concludes with a precise definition: if something is not inherently dependent on a subject, it is a substance; if it is in something that does depend on a subject, it is an accident. Throughout, the analysis uses technical logical terminology and is grounded in discussions of genera and species.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the syllogism: 'Every khamr (wine) is forbidden; every intoxicating drink is khamr; therefore every intoxicating drink is forbidden.' Identify a potential equivocation in the term 'khamr' (both a specific grape beverage and a general category). Does the syllogism commit the same fallacy as the one in the passage?
?
Construct a sound three-premise syllogism about the classification of a substance (e.g., 'The human is rational; rationality is not an accident; therefore human is not an accident') and test if it is valid.

The Ten Categories
intermediate
10 min
The Ten Categories as Highest Genera
Author argues highest genera are many, enumerates the ten categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the ten categories and explain why the highest genera are many, not one.

Core Idea
The highest genera are many; each category is a distinct and irreducible genus.

This passage establishes that there are ten highest genera, known as the categories. It corrects the misconception that there might be only one highest genus, and it clarifies how words like 'white' and 'three cubits' indicate categories indirectly. Understanding these categories is essential for classification in Islamic logic and metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the need for multiple highest genera
The author argues that since highest genera cannot have differentials, one might think there is only one. But the truth is they are many. Understand why: if many, they would need a universal encompassing them, which would require a differential, but they are highest so no differential. So they are irreducibly many.
This refutes the misconception that all categories derive from a single genus.
2
Memorize the list of ten categories
Commit to memory: substance (جوهر), quantity (كمية), quality (كيفية), relation (إضافة), place (أين), time (متى), position (وضع), state (جدة), action (أن يفعل), passion (أن ينفعل). Use the Arabic terms as they appear in the source.
These are the ten highest genera under which every being falls.
3
Distinguish direct name denotation from meaning indication
Note that words like 'white' (أبيض) denote a substance with whiteness, not the category quality directly. The category is the abstract meaning, like whiteness itself. Learn the difference between دلالة الاسم (name's denotation) and دلالة المعنى (meaning's indication).
This distinction prevents confusion between concrete instances and abstract categories.
4
Practice identifying categories from examples
Given examples like 'paternity', 'standing', 'cutting', determine which category they indicate. For each of the nine non-substance categories, find an example from the passage.
Use the author's own examples to reinforce the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأجناس العالية لما تبين أمرها بأنه لا يمكن أن تكون لها فصول مقومة. فلا يبعد أن يقع في الأوهام أن الجنس العالي واحد. ولو كان كثيرا لانحصرت في جامع يحوج إلى فصل بعده ، لكن الحق هو أن الأجناس العالية كثيرة. والمعاني المفردة التي تصلح أن يدل عليها بالألفاظ المفردة لا يخلو عن واحد منها : فإنها إما أن يدل على جوهر» أو على كمية» أو على «كيفية» أو على إضافة أو على أين ) أو على متى ) أو على وضع أو على جدة» أو على «أن يفعل» أو على «أن ينفعل». وهذه الأمثلة ليست تدل التسع منها على المقولة دلالة الاسم؛ بل يدل دلالة المعنى ؛ وذلك لأن أبيض مثلا ليس اسما للكيفية؛ بل اسما لشيء هو ذو كيفية وهو الجوهر لكن منه التنبيه على وجود الكيفية. فإن الأبيض كزيد مثلا أعرف عند التخيل من البياض الذي مجرد الكيفية، والتخيل أسبق إلينا في هذه الأمور من العقل، فإذا خطر ببالك الابيض، وذلك شيء ذو بياض فيدلك على البياض دلالة المعنى على المعنى والأمر على الأمر، فالمقولة ليست هي الأبيض ؛ بل البياض. وكذلك الكمية ليست هي شيئًا ذا ثلاثة أذرع مثلا؛ بل هي نفس طولها. وعليه هذا في البواقي فإن ما يدل على الجوهر من الألفاظ فذلك يدل على ذات فقط دلالة الاسم ولا يدل على أمر تنسب إليه الذات لا دلالة الاسم ولا دلالة المعنى. وأما إذا قلت بياض فإنه يدلك على معنى البياض دلالة الاسم، وقد يخطر ببالك الأبيض حينئذ أيضًا. وكذلك الحال في كل واحد من التسعة؛ فالمقولة منها هي ما يدل عليه المقدار والعدد والبياض والأبوة والكون في المكان كقولك الاتصال والانفصال والكون في الزمان كقولك العتاقة والحداثة والوضع كقولك القيام والجلوس. وأيضًا ما يدل عليه التسلح وصدور الفعل بالقطع وقبوله بالانقطاع ما دام يقطع.
Then, when it became clear about the highest genera that they cannot have constitutive differentials, it is not unlikely that one might imagine that the highest genus is one. And if it were many, they would be encompassed by a universal requiring a differential after it. But the truth is that the highest genera are many. The simple meanings that are suitable to be indicated by simple words cannot be free from one of them: for they either indicate substance, quantity, quality, relation, place, time, position, state, action, or passion. These examples do not indicate nine of them by way of the name's denotation, but by way of the meaning's indication. That is because 'white', for example, is not a name for quality; rather it is a name for something having a quality, which is a substance, but it alerts to the existence of quality. For 'white' like 'Zayd' is more known in imagination than whiteness, which is mere quality, and imagination is prior to us in these matters than intellect. So if 'white' comes to your mind, that is something having whiteness, and it indicates whiteness by the indication of one meaning to another, and one thing to another. So the category is not 'white'; rather it is whiteness. Similarly, quantity is not something having three cubits, for example; rather it is the length itself. And so on for the rest. For what indicates substance among the words, that indicates essence only by the name's denotation, and does not indicate something to which the essence is attributed, neither by name's denotation nor by meaning's indication. But if you say 'whiteness', it indicates to you the meaning of whiteness by name's denotation, and 'white' may come to your mind then as well. Similarly for each of the nine: the category among them is what is indicated by quantity, number, whiteness, paternity, being in place like your saying connection and separation, being in time like your saying antiquity and newness, position like your saying standing and sitting. Also what is indicated by arming, the issuing of action by cutting, and its reception by being cut as long as it cuts.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a definitional enumeration of the ten categories (al-maqūlāt al-‘ashr) as the highest genera. The author begins by noting that after establishing that highest genera have no constitutive differentials, one might mistakenly think there is only one highest genus. However, the truth is that there are many. He then lists the ten categories: substance (jawhar), quantity (kammiyya), quality (kayfiyya), relation (iḍāfa), place (ayn), time (matā), position (wad‘), state (jidda), action (an yaf‘al), and passion (an yanfa‘il). He clarifies that the examples given (like 'white' or 'three cubits') are not themselves the categories; they indicate the categories indirectly through meaning (dalālat al-ma‘nā) rather than directly through name (dalālat al-ism). For instance, 'white' is a name for a substance possessing whiteness, so it points to the category of quality indirectly. Similarly, 'three cubits' points to quantity, but quantity itself is the length. He explains that the category of substance is indicated by names that denote essence alone, without implying any attribute. For the other nine, examples are given: for quantity—magnitude and number; for quality—whiteness; for relation—paternity; for place—connection and separation (like being in place); for time—antiquity and newness; for position—standing and sitting; for action—arming and the issuing of an act like cutting; for passion—receiving cutting as long as cutting occurs. This passage is foundational for understanding Aristotelian categories as adopted in Islamic logic.

A) By name's denotation: 'white' directly names quality.
B) By meaning's indication: 'white' indicates a substance having whiteness, thus pointing to quality.
C) It does not indicate quality at all.
D) It indicates quantity instead.

Key Takeaway
The ten categories are the irreducible highest genera; they are many because they cannot be united under a single genus with a differential.

The Ten Categories
